Blind Person's Alternatives to Sight in Sales
Describes how a blind buyer may gain knowledge by touch, smell, taste

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the valid methods for a blind buyer to inspect goods and explain how each method lapses the khiyar al-ru'yah.

Core Idea
Blindness does not bar valid contracts; touch, smell, taste, and description substitute for sight.

The Hanafi school affirms that a blind person retains full contractual capacity and even enjoys khiyar al-ru'yah. However, since he cannot see, Sharia accommodates by allowing other sensory perceptions to replace sight. This lesson explores how a blind buyer gains knowledge of the item and when his option lapses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal capacity of a blind person
The passage confirms that a blind person's sale and purchase are valid, showing blindness does not affect ahliyyah.
2
List the sensory substitutes for sight
Touch, smell, and taste are stated as substitutes for sight, each applicable to different types of goods.
3
Apply the rule for real estate
For real estate, which cannot be fully perceived by senses, description suffices to lapse the option.
4
Determine when the option lapses
Once the blind buyer uses the appropriate method and is satisfied, the option of sight terminates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: إذا باع الأعمى أو اشترى، ما حكمه؟
الجواب: بيعه وشراؤه جائز، وله خيار الرؤية إذا اشترى.
السؤال: كيف يرى وهو لا يبصر؟
الجواب: جسه باليد إذا كان المبيع يعرف بالجس، وشمُّه إذا كان يدرك بالشم، وذوقه إذا كان يعرف بالذوق، يقوم مقام النظر.

السؤال: رجل لا يبصر وأراد أن يشتري العقار كيف يعرف؟
الجواب: يُكتفى له بالوصف في شراء العقار؛ لأنه لا سبيل له إلى معرفته إلا بذلك، فإذا وُصِفَ له ورضي بالشراء، سقط خياره.
Question: If a blind person sells or buys, what is the ruling? Answer: His sale and purchase are valid, and he has the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) when he buys. Question: How does he see when he is blind? Answer: He touches with his hand if the sold item is known by touch, and smells it if it is perceived by smell, and tastes it if it is known by taste; these stand in place of sight. Question: A man who is blind wants to buy real estate, how does he know? Answer: Description suffices for him in buying real estate because there is no way for him to know it except by that. So if it is described to him and he is pleased with the purchase, his option lapses.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage addresses the case of a blind person (a'ma) in sales. The Hanafi school affirms that a blind person's sale and purchase are valid. When he buys, he also has khiyar al-ru'yah, but since he cannot see, he uses other senses to gain knowledge of the object: touch for items known by feel (e.g., cloth), smell for perfumes or food, and taste for edibles. These senses substitute for sight and, if used with satisfaction, terminate the option. For real estate (real property), which cannot be fully perceived by touch/smell/taste, a description from a trustworthy person suffices. Once described and the buyer is satisfied, the option lapses. This shows the Hanafi flexibility in adapting the option principle to circumstances.

Key Terms
الأعمى (al-a'mā): A person who is blind; a legally blind person.
الجس (al-jass): Touch; the act of feeling with the hand to perceive qualities.
الشم (al-shamm): Smell; perceiving odor.
الذوق (al-dhawq): Taste; perceiving flavor.
الوصف (al-wasf): Description; a verbal account of the item.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option of sight; the right of a buyer to inspect the goods and cancel if not satisfied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
By what means does a blind buyer's khiyar al-ru'yah for real estate lapse?
A) Touching the walls
B) Smelling the soil
C) Receiving a description and being satisfied
D) It never lapses

Key Takeaway
A blind person's sale is valid; his khiyar al-ru'yah lapses by touch, smell, taste, or description for real estate.

Fuduli Sale: The Owner's Right to Ratify or Rescind
Rules on sale by non-owner; owner may ratify or void

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid ratification of a fuduli sale and identify the intra-Hanafi difference regarding knowledge of the item's existence.

Core Idea
A sale by a non-owner is suspended; owner may ratify only if the item and parties remain unchanged.

A fuduli sale—a contract made by someone who is neither the owner nor authorized—is not automatically void. Instead, it is suspended, giving the owner the right to either approve (ijāzah) or cancel (faskh). This lesson examines the precise conditions under which ratification is effective, and explores an internal Hanafi disagreement about whether the owner must know the item still exists at the time of ratification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the option holder
Determine that the owner of the sold item has the right to choose between ratification and rescission. This is the core of the fuduli sale.
2
List the conditions for valid ratification
From al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, you learn that four elements must still exist: the seller (the fuduli), the buyer, the owner, and the item itself. Ratification is only binding when all four are present.
These conditions apply at the moment of ratification.
3
Understand the effect of the owner's death
If the owner dies before ratifying, the sale dissolves automatically and the heirs cannot ratify. This shows that the right is personal.
4
Examine the condition that the item exists and the parties remain
The text states that ratification is valid only if the item still exists and the original contracting parties are alive and unchanged. This is a key condition.
The commentary expands that if the item's existence is uncertain, a legal presumption (aṣl) applies.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad holds that ratification is valid even if the item's existence is unknown, because the legal principle (aṣl) presumes it still exists. Imam Abu Yusuf requires knowledge of existence, arguing that doubt in a condition invalidates it.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; later preferred view may vary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: رجل باع ملك غيره بغير أمره، ما حكمه؟
الجواب: المالك محتار في هذه الصورة، إن شاء أجاز البيع، وإن شاء فسخ، وله حق الإجازة إذا كان المعقود عليه باقيا، والمتعاقدان بحالها.

ببحالها: قال في الجوهرة النيرة: اعلم أن قيام الأربعة شرط للحوق الإجازة البائع والمشتري والمالك والمبيع، فإن أجاز المالك مع قيام هذه الأربعة جاز إلى أن قال وإن لم يجز المالك البيع وفسخه انفسخ ويرجع المشتري على البائع، فإن مات المالك قبل الإجازة انفسخ البيع ولا يجوز بإجازة ورثته. قوله: إذا كان المعقود عليه باقيا والمتعاقدان بحالهما، وإذا لم يعلم حال المبيع، باق هو أم هالك صحت الإجازة؛ لأن الأصل بقاؤه، وهذا قول محمد وقال أبو يوسف لا يصح حتى يعلم قيامه وقت الإجازة؛ لأن الشك وقع في شرط الإجازة فلا يثبت مع الشك. وهذه المسألة تتعلق ببيع الفضولي، لكن ذكرها القدوري ههنا فاتبعناه.
Question: A man sold someone else's property without his permission, what is the ruling? Answer: The owner has the choice in this case: if he wishes, he may ratify the sale, and if he wishes, he may rescind. He has the right of ratification only if the subject matter still exists and the two contracting parties remain unchanged. 

From al-Jawhara al-Nayyira: 'Know that the existence of four things is a condition for the binding of ratification: the seller, the buyer, the owner, and the sold item. If the owner ratifies while these four exist, it is valid... and if the owner does not ratify the sale and rescinds it, it is rescinded, and the buyer recovers from the seller. If the owner dies before ratification, the sale is rescinded and cannot be ratified by his heirs.' His statement: 'if the subject matter still exists and the two contracting parties are as they were' — and if the condition of the sold item is unknown, whether it still exists or is destroyed, ratification is valid because the general principle (aṣl) is its continued existence. This is the view of Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: 'It is not valid until he knows its existence at the time of ratification, because doubt has occurred regarding a condition of ratification, so it is not established with doubt.' This issue belongs to the sale of a non-owner (bay' al-fuḍūlī), but al-Quduri mentioned it here, so we followed him.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage discusses the fuduli sale – a contract made by a person who is not the owner and without authorization. The owner has the right to ratify (ijāzah) or rescind (faskh). Ratification is valid only if the sold item remains in existence and the original contracting parties (seller and buyer) are still alive and unchanged. The commentary from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira adds that four things must exist for ratification: the seller (the fuduli), the buyer, the owner, and the item. If the owner dies before ratifying, the sale becomes void and his heirs cannot ratify. An intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Imam Muhammad holds that if the existence of the item is unknown, ratification is valid because the legal presumption (aṣl) is that it still exists. Imam Abu Yusuf requires certainty, citing doubt. The mufta bihi position is not explicitly stated here, but the text follows al-Quduri's placement.

Key Terms
بيع الفضولي (bay' al-fuḍūlī): A sale made by a person who is neither the owner nor authorized by the owner.
الإجازة (al-ijāzah): Ratification of a contract by the owner after it was made by a non-owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what happens if the owner of property sold by a fuduli dies before ratifying?
A) The heirs may ratify
B) The sale is void
C) The sale is valid automatically
D) The buyer takes possession anyway

Key Takeaway
A fuduli sale is not void but suspended; the owner may ratify only if the item and parties remain, and a Hanafi internal difference exists on whether knowledge of the item's existence is required.

Two Garments in One Contract: Right to Return Both
Buyer may return both garments even if only one was unseen

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer purchases two items in a single contract and had not seen one at the time of contract, and explain the basis for returning both.

Core Idea
In a single contract, the option of sight attaches to the entire contract, allowing return of all items.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in sales law: a buyer purchases two items in one contract but had not seen one of them at the time of sale. Although he later sees both, the question arises whether he can return both. The Hanafi ruling, based on the principle of contract indivisibility for the option of sight, permits returning both.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Recognize that the two garments were bought in a single contract ( عقـد واحـد ). This is the key condition.
If the items were in separate contracts, the ruling differs.
2
Determine the status of sight at contract time
The buyer had seen one garment but not the other at the time of contract. This triggers khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) for the unseen item.
Khiyar al-ru'yah is a recognized right in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the rule of contract indivisibility
Because the contract is single, the option of sight attaches to the entire contract, not just the unseen item.
The Hanafi school treats the contract as an indivisible unit for this purpose.
4
State the ruling and its basis
The buyer may return both garments. The basis is avoiding hardship ( harm ) and the unity of the contract.
The ruling is permissive ( ja'iz ) — the buyer has the choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: رجل اشترى ثوبين في عقد واحد، وقد رأى أحدهما ثم رأى الآخر، هل يجوز له ردهما؟
الجواب: نعم، يجوز له ذلك.
Question: A man bought two garments in a single contract; he had seen one of them, then he saw the other. Is it permissible for him to return both? Answer: Yes, it is permissible for him to do so.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This ruling addresses a scenario where two items are purchased in a single contract, but the buyer had not seen one of them at the time of contract. Although he later sees both, he may return both because the contract is indivisible for khiyar purposes. The option of sight attaches to the contract as a whole; if one item was bought unseen, the buyer may rescind the entire contract. This prevents hardship from being forced to keep one item due to a single contract.

Key Terms
كِثْيَارُ الرُّؤْيَة (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of sight — a buyer's right to rescind a contract after seeing an item that was unseen at the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases two items in one contract, having seen only one, what can he do after seeing the other?
A) Return only the unseen item
B) Return both items
C) Keep both and ask for a discount
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
In a single contract of sale, the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) applies to all items, allowing the buyer to return the whole purchase if any item was unseen.

Effect of Time Gap on Khiyar al-Ru'yah
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah after time gap

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for khiyar al-ru'yah after a time delay and apply it to determine whether the buyer has an option.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'yah after delay: unchanged item means no option; changed item revives the option.

When a buyer sees an item and later purchases it without a fresh inspection, is the right of sight still available? The answer depends on whether the item has changed in the interim. This lesson clarifies the condition that triggers or negates the option.

Guided Path
1
Confirm prior sighting
Establish that the buyer had actually seen the item before the purchase contract.
The option only applies if the buyer has not seen the item at the time of contract.
2
Determine the condition at delivery
Compare the item's condition when received to its condition at the time of prior sighting.
The change must be material (e.g., damage, alteration in quality).
3
Apply the ruling
If the item is unchanged (bāqiyan 'alā al-ṣifah), no option exists. If changed (mutaghayyiran), the buyer has khiyar al-ru'yah.
The burden of proving change falls on the buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: رأى شيئا ثم اشتراه بعد مدة، هل يثبت له خيار الرؤية؟
الجواب: إن كان هذا الشيء باقيا على الصفة التي رآه قبل ذلك، فلا خيار له، وإن وجده متغيرا فله الخيار.
Question: He saw something and then bought it after some time; does he have the option of sight? Answer: If this thing remains in the condition in which he saw it before, then he has no option; but if he finds it changed, then he has the option.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage clarifies the effect of a time gap between seeing an object and purchasing it. If the item remains unchanged (bāqī ‘alā al-ṣifah) from the earlier sighting, the khiyar al-ru'yah does not arise because the buyer already had sufficient knowledge. However, if the item has changed (mutaghayyiran) in a way that affects its value or description, the buyer has the option because his previous knowledge no longer applies. The option is linked to the state of the item at purchase.

Key Terms
باقيا على الصفة (bāqiyan 'alā al-ṣifah): Remaining in the same condition as previously seen.
متغيرا (mutaghayyiran): Changed from the previous condition in a material way.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does a buyer not have khiyar al-ru'yah after seeing an item and buying it later?
A) If he forgot its condition
B) If it remains unchanged
C) Always
D) If the seller is the same person

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'yah after a delay depends solely on whether the item has materially changed; unchanged means no option, changed means option.

Death of Buyer: Khiyar al-Ru'yah Extinguished
Ruling that khiyar al-ru'yah ends at buyer's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah when the buyer dies and explain why it does not transfer to heirs.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'yah is a personal right that dies with the buyer.

When a buyer purchases an unseen item, Islamic law grants him the right to rescind the contract upon seeing it. But what happens if the buyer dies before exercising that right? This lesson clarifies that the option is a personal entitlement, not a transferable property right; therefore, it lapses upon death and the sale becomes binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight). The ruling is that upon the buyer's death, the option becomes void and the sale is finalized.
2
Trace the legal basis
Understand that khiyar al-ru'yah is a right (haqq) given to remove the buyer's ignorance about the unseen item. It is personal to the buyer.
The right is not financial but personal; hence it does not pass to heirs.
3
Apply the ruling to the question
The source states: 'tamma al-bay‘ wa baṭala al-khiyār, wa lā yantaqilu ilā warathatih.' (The sale is finalized, the option becomes void, and it does not transfer to his heirs.)
The heirs cannot exercise the option; they inherit the sold item as it is.
4
Distinguish from other khiyar types
Compare with khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) which may transfer if explicitly agreed upon. This highlights the unique personal nature of khiyar al-ru'yah.
The difference lies in the origin: one is a legal right, the other a contractual term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: مات المشتري، وكان له خيار الرؤية، ما حكم الخيار بعد موته؟
الجواب: تم البيع وبطل الخيار، ولا ينتقل إلى ورثته.
Question: The buyer died, and he had the option of sight. What is the ruling of the option after his death? Answer: The sale is finalized and the option becomes void, and it does not transfer to his heirs.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This ruling establishes that khiyar al-ru'yah is a personal right (ḥaqq shakhṣī) that does not survive the buyer. Upon the buyer's death, the contract becomes binding (lāzim) and the option lapses. It does not pass to the heirs as part of the estate. This is because the option was granted to remove ignorance specific to the buyer; the heirs are considered to have accepted the contract through inheritance, or the contract is deemed finalized.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru’yah): The option of sight: a legal right allowing the buyer to cancel a contract after seeing the purchased item if it was bought unseen.
تم البيع (tamma al-bay‘): The sale is finalized: the contract becomes binding and irrevocable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to khiyar al-ru'yah when the buyer dies?
A) It passes to his heirs
B) The sale is voided automatically
C) The option is extinguished and sale is finalized
D) The seller may choose to terminate

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'yah is a personal right that ends with the buyer's death; the sale becomes binding and the option does not transfer to heirs.

Rulings on Khiyar al-Ru'ya (Option of Sight)
Q&A rulings on khiyar al-ru'ya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on khiyar al-ru'ya for each scenario: blind purchase, description-based real estate, multiple items in one contract, delayed purchase, and buyer's death.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'ya is a personal right; it can be waived by description or lapse of time, but it does not transfer to heirs.

The option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) gives the buyer the right to cancel a sale if he has not seen the item. This lesson covers several scenarios from the Hanafi text: how a blind person can exercise this option, how description substitutes for sight in real estate, the effect of seeing one of multiple items, the effect of delay, and what happens if the buyer dies. Understanding these rulings clarifies the boundaries of this important consumer protection.

Guided Path
1
Identify each question's scenario
The passage presents six distinct scenarios in a Q&A format. Read each question carefully to understand the situation, such as a blind buyer, a sale of two garments, a delayed purchase, etc.
Each scenario is independent; the ruling applies to that specific fact pattern.
2
Extract the Hanafi ruling
For each scenario, note the ruling given by the text. The answer begins with 'الجواب' and states whether the option exists or is waived.
Write down the ruling in your own words, e.g., 'the option is waived for the blind buyer if description suffices'.
3
Understand the rationale
The text does not always explicitly state the reason, but you can infer: for the blind, description is the only means of knowledge, so it substitutes for sight; for multiple items in one contract, the option attaches to the entire contract; for delay, the option is lost if the item remains unchanged; for death, the option ends because it is personal.
Think about the underlying principle: khiyar al-ru'ya protects against unseen defects, but knowledge through any reliable means waives it.
4
Apply to a similar scenario
Test your understanding by creating a variant of one scenario. For example, what if a blind person buys a car after description? Or what if the buyer dies after seeing the item?
The key is to identify which condition (sight, description, delay, death) triggers the ruling.
5
Summarize the conditions for khiyar al-ru'ya
From the rulings, compile a list of what gives rise to the option (e.g., never seeing the item, change in item after seeing) and what waives it (e.g., seeing, valid description, no change after delay, death of buyer).
This summary will be useful for quick reference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خيار الرؤية
كتاب البيوع الجواب: جسه باليد إذا كان المبيع يعرف بالجس، وشمُّه إذا كان يدرك بالشم، وذوقه إذا كان يعرف بالذوق، يقوم مقام النظر.
السؤال: رجل لا يبصر وأراد أن يشتري العقار كيف يعرف؟
الجواب : يُكتفى له بالوصف في شراء العقار؛ لأنه لا سبيل له إلى معرفته إلا بذلك، فإذا وُصِفَ له ورضي بالشراء، سقط خياره.
السؤال: رجل باع ملك غيره بغير أمره، ما حكمه؟ الجواب : المالك محتار في هذه الصورة، إن شاء أجاز البيع، وإن شاء فسخ، وله حق الإجازة إذا كان المعقود عليه باقيا، والمتعاقدان بحالها.
السؤال: رجل اشترى ثوبين في عقد واحد، وقد رأى أحدهما ثم رأى الآخر، هل يجوز له ردهما؟ الجواب: نعم، يجوز له ذلك.
السؤال رأى شيئا ثم اشتراه بعد مدة، هل يثبت له خيار الرؤية؟
الجواب: إن كان هذا الشيء باقيا على الصفة التي رآه قبل ذلك، فلا خيار له، وإن وجده متغيرا فله الخيار
السؤال: مات المشتري، وكان له خيار الرؤية، ما حكم الخيار بعد موته؟
الجواب: تم البيع وبطل الخيار، ولا ينتقل إلى ورثته.
بحالهما: قال في "الجوهرة النيرة": اعلم أن قيام الأربعة شرط للحوق الإجازة البائع والمشتري والمالك والمبيع، فإن أجاز المالك مع قيام هذه الأربعة جاز إلى أن قال وإن لم يجز المالك البيع وفسخه انفسخ ويرجع المشتري على البائع، فإن مات المالك قبل الإجازة انفسخ البيع ولا يجوز بإجازة ورثته. قوله: "إذا كان المعقود عليه باقيا والمتعاقدان بحالهما ، وإذا لم يعلم حال المبيع، باق هو أم هالك صحت الإجازة؛ لأن الأصل بقاؤه، وهذا قول محمد وقال أبو يوسف لا يصح حتى يعلم قيامه وقت الإجازة؛ لأن الشك وقع في شرط الإجازة فلا يثبت مع الشك. وهذه المسألة تتعلق ببيع الفضولي، لكن ذكرها القدوري ههنا فاتبعناه.
Option of Sight, Book of Sales: The answer: Feel it with the hand if the sold item is known by feeling, and smell it if it is perceived by smell, and taste it if it is known by taste; it takes the place of sight. Question: A man who is blind wants to buy real estate, how does he know? Answer: Description suffices for him in purchasing real estate because he has no way to know it except by that; so if it is described to him and he is satisfied with the purchase, his option is waived. Question: A man sold the property of another without his permission, what is the ruling? Answer: The owner has discretion in this case: if he wishes, he ratifies the sale, and if he wishes, he rescinds it. He has the right of ratification if the object of contract still exists and the two contracting parties remain as they were. Question: A man bought two garments in one contract, and he saw one then saw the other, is it permissible for him to return both? Answer: Yes, it is permissible for him to do so. Question: He saw something and then bought it after a period, is the option of sight established for him? Answer: If that thing remains in the condition he saw it before, then there is no option for him; but if he finds it changed, then he has the option. Question: The buyer died while he had the option of sight, what is the ruling on the option after his death? Answer: The sale is complete and the option is void, and it does not transfer to his heirs. Commentary: He said in al-Jawhara al-Nayyira: Know that the existence of four things is a condition for the validity of ratification: the seller, the buyer, the owner, and the object sold. If the owner ratifies with these four existing, it is valid... until he said: And if the owner does not ratify the sale and rescinds it, it is rescinded, and the buyer seeks recourse from the seller. If the owner dies before ratification, the sale is rescinded and the ratification of his heirs is not valid. His statement: 'if the object of contract still exists and the two contracting parties remain as they were' – and if the condition of the sold item is not known, whether it remains or is destroyed, the ratification is valid because the original presumption is its continued existence. This is the view of Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: It is not valid until he knows of its existence at the time of ratification because doubt arises concerning the condition of ratification, so it cannot be established with doubt. This issue relates to the sale by a non-owner, but al-Quduri mentioned it here so we follow him.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage, presented in a question-answer format, addresses the option of sight and related rulings in sales. It begins by explaining that for a blind buyer, touching, smelling, or tasting can substitute for sight, and if the item is described and accepted, the option is lost. For a fuduli sale (sale of another's property without permission), the owner has the choice to ratify or rescind, provided the item and parties remain unchanged. If someone buys two items in one contract and sees one, he may return both if he later sees the other. The option of sight is lost if the buyer purchases after a delay with no change in the item; if changed, he may exercise the option. On the buyer's death, the option terminates and does not pass to heirs. The commentary from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira elaborates on the conditions for ratification in fuduli sales: the existence of the seller, buyer, owner, and sold item is necessary. A dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf is noted: Muhammad holds that ratification is valid even if the item's existence is unknown due to the legal presumption of continuity, while Abu Yusuf requires actual knowledge. This discussion is properly part of fuduli sales but is included here following al-Quduri's arrangement.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of the buyer to rescind the sale if he has not seen the item or if it is found different from the description that constituted sight.
بيع الفضولي (bay' al-fuduli): A sale conducted by a person without the owner's permission, subject to the owner's ratification.
إجازة (ijaza): Ratification of a contract by the owner in a fuduli sale, rendering it valid retroactively from the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer dies after purchasing an item with the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) but before seeing it, what is the ruling?
A) The option transfers to his heirs.
B) The sale is void.
C) The sale is complete and the option is lost.
D) The buyer must be informed of the death.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'ya is a personal right that can be waived by description, lapse of time without change, or death; it does not transfer to heirs.

Fuduli Sale: Conditions for Ratification and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on fuduli sale conditions and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for ratification of a fuduli sale and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the presumption of existence.

Core Idea
Ratification requires existence of parties and object; but uncertainty about the object's existence leads to a dispute: Imam Muhammad relies on presumption, Abu Yusuf requires certainty.

In a fuduli sale (sale of another's property without permission), the owner's ratification (ijaza) can make the contract valid. However, certain conditions must be met. This lesson examines the conditions outlined in al-Quduri's text and the commentary from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, including the dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on whether ratification is valid when the existence of the sold item is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Define fuduli sale and the role of ratification
A fuduli sale is a sale conducted by a non-owner without the owner's permission. Ratification (ijaza) by the owner can make the sale valid retroactively. Understand that without ratifications, the sale is not binding on the owner.
The owner has a choice: to ratify or rescind.
2
List the four conditions for validity of ratification
From al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, the text states that the existence of four things is a condition: the seller, the buyer, the owner, and the object sold. Also, the object and parties must remain unchanged.
These conditions must be met at the time of ratification.
3
Introduce the condition of object's existence
The condition that the object be 'still existing' raises a question: what if we do not know whether the object exists at the time of ratification? This leads to the dispute.
Focus on the phrase 'if the object of contract still exists' and the commentary's expansion.
4
Compare the two positions
Imam Muhammad holds that ratification is valid even if existence is unknown, because the legal presumption (istishab) is that the object continues to exist. Abu Yusuf holds that ratification requires actual knowledge of existence, because doubt (shakk) in a condition cannot establish it.
Note the thamarah al-khilaf: if the object is later found destroyed, under Muhammad the ratification may be voided; under Abu Yusuf it was invalid from start.
5
Determine the practical implication
Later Hanafi practice may act on both views in different contexts. The mufti should be aware of both positions and may choose based on circumstances.
This dispute is a legitimate intra-madhhab difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خيار الرؤية
كتاب البيوع الجواب: جسه باليد إذا كان المبيع يعرف بالجس، وشمُّه إذا كان يدرك بالشم، وذوقه إذا كان يعرف بالذوق، يقوم مقام النظر.
السؤال: رجل لا يبصر وأراد أن يشتري العقار كيف يعرف؟
الجواب : يُكتفى له بالوصف في شراء العقار؛ لأنه لا سبيل له إلى معرفته إلا بذلك، فإذا وُصِفَ له ورضي بالشراء، سقط خياره.
السؤال: رجل باع ملك غيره بغير أمره، ما حكمه؟ الجواب : المالك محتار في هذه الصورة، إن شاء أجاز البيع، وإن شاء فسخ، وله حق الإجازة إذا كان المعقود عليه باقيا، والمتعاقدان بحالها.
السؤال: رجل اشترى ثوبين في عقد واحد، وقد رأى أحدهما ثم رأى الآخر، هل يجوز له ردهما؟ الجواب: نعم، يجوز له ذلك.
السؤال رأى شيئا ثم اشتراه بعد مدة، هل يثبت له خيار الرؤية؟
الجواب: إن كان هذا الشيء باقيا على الصفة التي رآه قبل ذلك، فلا خيار له، وإن وجده متغيرا فله الخيار
السؤال: مات المشتري، وكان له خيار الرؤية، ما حكم الخيار بعد موته؟
الجواب: تم البيع وبطل الخيار، ولا ينتقل إلى ورثته.
بحالهما: قال في "الجوهرة النيرة": اعلم أن قيام الأربعة شرط للحوق الإجازة البائع والمشتري والمالك والمبيع، فإن أجاز المالك مع قيام هذه الأربعة جاز إلى أن قال وإن لم يجز المالك البيع وفسخه انفسخ ويرجع المشتري على البائع، فإن مات المالك قبل الإجازة انفسخ البيع ولا يجوز بإجازة ورثته. قوله: "إذا كان المعقود عليه باقيا والمتعاقدان بحالهما ، وإذا لم يعلم حال المبيع، باق هو أم هالك صحت الإجازة؛ لأن الأصل بقاؤه، وهذا قول محمد وقال أبو يوسف لا يصح حتى يعلم قيامه وقت الإجازة؛ لأن الشك وقع في شرط الإجازة فلا يثبت مع الشك. وهذه المسألة تتعلق ببيع الفضولي، لكن ذكرها القدوري ههنا فاتبعناه.

The Option of Defect (Khiyar al-Ayb) in Hanafi Law
Q&A ruling on khiyar al-ayb with definitions and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for khiyar al-ayb and apply the ruling: a buyer may return or keep the item, but not claim a reduction.

Core Idea
A buyer with a hidden defect can only return the item or keep it at full price — no partial refund.

This passage, structured as a Q&A, introduces the Hanafi ruling on the option of defect. It establishes the buyer's right to return an item if the defect was pre-existing, hidden, and undisclosed. The text also defines what constitutes a defect and provides specific examples for slaves, emphasizing that custom determines the standard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the buyer's options
From the answer to the first question, note the buyer may either keep the item at full price or return it. There is no third option.
This is a binary choice.
2
Note the prohibition on reduction
The second answer explicitly denies the buyer's right to ask for a partial refund (arsh). This is a key Hanafi distinction.
Other schools allow arsh.
3
Understand the customary definition of defect
A defect is anything that reduces the price according to merchant custom. This is a flexible, custom-based standard.
No fixed list; depends on trade norms.
4
Analyze examples of defects
The examples show that defect status depends on the type of slave (age, gender) and social context. Running away is a defect in a minor slave but not in an adult.
Custom and context matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: اشترى رجل شيئا ثم اطلع فيه على عيب كان عند البائع، هل يجوز له أن يرد المبيع؟ الجواب: إن كان المشتري لم ير العيب عند البيع ولا عند القبض ولم يبينه البائع، فهو بالخيار إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن وإن شاء رده. السؤال: أليس للمشترى أن يمسكه ويأخذ النقصان؟ الجواب: ليس له ذلك. السؤال: أي عيب يعتبر في هذه المسائل؟ الجواب: كل ما أوجب نقصان الثمن في عادة التجار فهو عيب السؤال: بينوا بعض العيوب التي جاز للمشتري أن يرد المبيع بسببها. الجواب: الإباق، والبول في الفراش، والسرقة عيب في الصغير ما لم يبلغ، فإذا بلغ فليس ذلك بعيب، والبخر والذفر عيب في الجارية، وليس بعيب في الغلام إلا أن يكون من داء، والزنا وكون المملوك ولد الزنا عيب في الجارية دون الغلام
Question: A man bought something then discovered a defect that existed with the seller. Is it permissible for him to return the sold item? Answer: If the buyer did not see the defect at the time of sale nor at the time of possession, and the seller did not disclose it, then he has the option: if he wishes he may take it for the full price, and if he wishes he may return it. Question: Does he not have the right to keep it and take a reduction? Answer: He does not have that. Question: What defect is considered in these matters? Answer: Everything that causes a decrease in the price according to merchant custom is a defect. Question: Mention some defects for which it is permissible for the buyer to return the sold item. Answer: Running away, urinating in bed, theft are defects in a minor as long as he has not reached puberty; if he reaches puberty then that is not a defect. Halitosis and foul odor are defects in a female slave, but not in a male slave unless it is from a disease. Adultery and the slave being born of adultery are defects in a female slave but not in a male slave.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This is a ruling statement defining the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) and its application. The passage is structured as a series of questions and answers. The first question establishes the basic right: if a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect that was hidden, he has the choice to either keep the item at the full price or return it. The second question clarifies that he cannot demand a partial refund; he must either take it unconditionally or return it. The third question defines a defect according to the Hanafi school: anything that reduces the price in the custom of merchants. Note that this is a customary definition, not a fixed legal list. The fourth question gives examples: running away (abaq), urinating in bed, and theft are defects in a minor slave (one who has not reached puberty) but not after puberty because the cause changes (weakness vs. maliciousness). Halitosis and foul odor are defects in a female slave (where beauty is a factor) but not in a male slave unless from disease. Adultery and illegitimate birth are defects in a female slave (affecting value) but not in a male. These examples show that defects are not absolute but depend on the type of slave and societal norms.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The option to return a sold item due to a pre-existing defect
عيب (ayb): Any defect that reduces the price according to merchant custom

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of a defect (ayb) in sales?
A) Any physical flaw in the item
B) Everything that causes a decrease in price according to merchant custom
C) Any hidden flaw that the seller knew about
D) Anything that makes the item unusable

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, a hidden defect gives the buyer only two options: return the item or keep it at full price, never a partial reduction.

Age-Based Variation in Defects of Slaves
Explains how defects vary with the slave's age at occurrence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the same physical defect may have different causes depending on the slave's age, and determine whether the buyer has the right to return the slave.

Core Idea
A defect is not a fixed physical event; its legal effect depends on the underlying cause (sabab), which changes with age.

In Hanafi law, the right to return a slave due to a defect (khiyar al-ayb) requires that the defect existed at the time of sale. However, certain behaviors like bed-wetting, running away, and theft may be considered defects only when they occur at a specific age, because the cause differs. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this distinction, drawing from the works of al-Hidayah and al-Bahr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defects and their possible causes
List the three defects mentioned: bed-wetting, running away, and theft. Note that each can have two distinct causes depending on the slave's age: childhood (weakness, love of play, lack of care) and adulthood (internal disease, malice).
2
Understand the distinction between childhood and adulthood causes
The passage emphasizes that the same behavior in childhood and adulthood arises from different physical or psychological conditions. This difference is legally significant because the cause (sabab) determines the classification as a defect.
3
Apply the rule of 'unity of cause'
For the buyer to return the slave, the defect must have the same cause during both the seller's and buyer's ownership. That means the behavior must have occurred at the same life stage (both childhood or both adulthood) with both parties.
4
Determine the ruling based on the age
If the behavior occurred in childhood with the seller and in adulthood with the buyer, the buyer cannot return the slave because the cause is different. If it occurred in the same stage with both, the buyer may return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب لأنه فإذا بلغ: قال صاحب "الهداية": معناه إذا ظهرت هذه الأشياء عند البائع في صغره ثم حدثت عند المشتري في صغره فله أن یرده عين ذلك، وإن حدثت بعد بلوغه أي عند المشتري، لم يرده؛ لأنه غيره، وهذا لأن هذه الأشياء يختلف بالصغر والكبر، فالبول في الفراش في الصغر لضعف المثانة وبعد الكبر لداء في الباطن، والإباق في الصغر لحب اللعب، والسرقة لقلة ،المبالاة، وهما بعد الكبر لخبث في الباطن. والمراد من الصغير من يعقل، فأما الذي لا يعقل فهو ضال لا أبق فلا يتحقق عيبا، قال صاحب "البحر" (٤٣/٦ ) : فإن بال في الصغر عند البائع ثم بعد البلوغ عند المشتري لا يرده، بخلاف ما إذا بال عندهما في الصغر أو في الكبر فإنه يرده لاتحاد السبب. وقال في ذكر السرقة (٤٤/٦): ولا بد من المعاودة عند المشتري في حالة واحدة، فلا بد من السرقة عندهما في الصغر أو بعد البلوغ، فإن سرق عند البائع في صغره ثم عند المشتري بعد بلوغه، لا يرده لحدوث العيب؛ لأن في الصغر لقلة المبالاة وفي الكبر لخبث في الباطن.
Because: when [the slave] reaches puberty, the author of al-Hidayah said: meaning if these things appeared with the seller in his childhood then occurred with the buyer in his childhood, he may return it accordingly; but if they occurred after puberty, i.e., with the buyer, he may not return it because it is different. This is because these things differ with childhood and adulthood: urinating in bed in childhood is due to weakness of the bladder, and after adulthood due to internal disease; running away in childhood due to love of play, and theft due to lack of care; both after adulthood due to inner malice. What is meant by minor is one who possesses reason; as for one who does not possess reason, he is astray, not a runaway, so no defect is established. The author of al-Bahr said (6/43): If he urinated in childhood with the seller, then after puberty with the buyer, he may not return it, unlike if he urinated with both in childhood or both in adulthood, then he returns it due to unity of cause. And regarding theft (6/44): Recurrence with the buyer is necessary in one case; theft must occur with both in childhood or after puberty; if he stole with the seller in his childhood then with the buyer after his puberty, he may not return it due to the occurrence of a defect; because in childhood it is due to lack of care, and in adulthood due to inner malice.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an expansion and reasoning for the earlier ruling that certain defects (urinating, running away, theft) are considered defects only in minors. The author quotes al-Hidayah and al-Bahr to explain the underlying cause ('illa): these actions in childhood arise from immaturity or weakness, whereas in adulthood they reflect character flaws or disease. Therefore, if the same behavior occurs in both periods, the cause is different, so the buyer cannot attribute it to the seller unless the behavior occurred consistently (at the same stage) with both parties. For example, if a slave urinated in bed as a child with the seller, and then as an adult with the buyer, the buyer cannot return him because the adult behavior is a new defect not present with the seller. But if the slave urinated in bed as a child at both places, the buyer can return because the cause is the same (childhood weakness). The text also clarifies that 'minor' here means a child who understands; a non-rational child like a toddler who wanders is not considered a runaway, so it is not a defect. This detailed analogical reasoning (qiyas) is typical of Hanafi legal methodology.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): The reason or cause for the legal ruling
صغير (saghir): A minor who has not reached puberty but has some understanding

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave urinated in bed with the seller as a child and then urinated in bed with the buyer as an adult. What is the ruling according to al-Bahr?
A) The buyer may return the slave
B) The buyer may not return the slave because the cause differs
C) The buyer must accept a reduction in price
D) The buyer may only return if both urinations were in childhood

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the same physical behavior in a slave can have different legal effects depending on whether it occurs in childhood or adulthood because the underlying cause (sabab) differs; only when the cause is consistent across ownership can the buyer exercise the right to return.

Buyer's Recourse When He Causes a Defect After Purchase
Buyer's defect after purchase bars return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer discovers a seller's defect after causing a new defect, and distinguish between the buyer's right to return vs. claim compensation.

Core Idea
A buyer who causes a new defect cannot return the item — only claim compensation for the seller's original defect.

Imagine you buy a used phone, accidentally drop and crack the screen, then discover the phone had a defective battery that the seller did not disclose. Can you return the phone for a full refund? According to the Hanafi school, you cannot, because your own action has altered the item. Instead, you may only claim the reduction in value caused by the seller's defect. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The buyer causes a new defect (e.g., breaks the item) before discovering a pre-existing defect from the seller.
The order matters: the buyer's defect occurs first, then the seller's defect is discovered.
2
State the default ruling
The buyer cannot return the item because he altered it. He may only claim compensation (naqsan al-ayb) for the seller's defect.
Compensation equals the difference in value caused by the seller's defect.
3
Note the exception
If the seller agrees to take back the item despite the new defect, return is allowed by mutual consent.
This is a bilateral agreement, not a right.
4
Understand the rationale
The rule protects the seller from receiving a damaged item he did not cause, while still allowing the buyer to recover losses from the seller's defect.
Balances fairness between buyer and seller.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال حدث عيب عند المشتري ثم اطلع على عيب كان عند البائع، ماذا يفعل الآن؟ الجواب له أن يرجع بنقصان العيب، ولا يرد المبيع إلا أن يرضى البائع أن يأخذه بعيبه الذي حدث عند المشتري.
Question: A defect occurred with the buyer, then he discovered a defect that existed with the seller. What should he do now? Answer: He may claim compensation for the defect, and he may not return the sold item unless the seller agrees to take it with the defect that occurred with the buyer.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This ruling addresses a scenario where the buyer causes a new defect (e.g., breaking the item) after purchase, but later discovers a pre-existing defect from the seller. Since the item is now altered by the buyer's action, the buyer cannot return it in its original state. The Hanafi rule is that the buyer can only claim the difference in value (naqsan al-ayb) from the seller. However, if the seller is willing to take back the item with the new defect, that is permissible by mutual agreement. This protects the seller from receiving a damaged item that he did not cause.

Key Terms
نقصان العيب (naqsan al-ayb): Monetary compensation equal to the reduction in market value caused by the seller's defect, claimed by the buyer when return is not possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect after he has already caused a new defect to the item, what is his recourse under Hanafi law?
A) He may return the item and demand a full refund.
B) He may claim compensation for the seller's defect only.
C) He may keep the item and claim a partial refund for both defects.
D) He has no recourse because he caused a new defect.

Key Takeaway
A buyer who causes a new defect loses the right to return the item and may only claim compensation for the seller's original defect.

Ruling When Buyer Alters the Item After Purchase
Rules when buyer changes the item's form after purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer alters a defective item and apply it to determine whether return or compensation is possible.

Core Idea
Alteration of the item forfeits the right of return; only compensation is due.

When you buy something and later find a defect, you normally have the option to return it. But what if you have already cut, sewed, dyed, or mixed the item? The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling: the buyer loses the right of return and may only claim compensation for the reduction in value caused by the defect. This lesson explores that rule and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the defect ruling
Recognize that the buyer has discovered a defect that existed at the time of sale. Normally, this would trigger khiyar al-ayb (option of defect), allowing the buyer to return the item or claim compensation.
The defect must pre-exist the alteration.
2
Recognize the alteration that prevents return
The buyer has performed an act that changes the item's form—cutting and sewing, dyeing, or mixing. Such alteration makes the item no longer identical to what was delivered.
The text lists specific examples: cutting+sewing, dyeing, mixing saweeq with ghee.
3
Understand the consequence: only compensation
Because the item is altered, the right of return is forfeited. The remedy is limited to claiming compensation (naqsan) for the reduction in value caused by the defect.
The ruling is 'رجع بنقصانه' – he claims compensation for the reduction.
4
Note that the seller cannot demand the altered item back
The seller has no right to reclaim the altered item even if he wants it. He can only receive the compensation amount.
The text says: 'وليس للبائع أن يأخذه بعينه' – the seller may not take it back (as is).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال اشترى ثوبا وقطعه وخاطه أو صبغه، أو اشترى سويقا فلته بسمن -أي خلطه به - ثم اطلع على عيب كان عند البائع، ما حكمه؟ الجواب: رجع بنقصانه، وليس للبائع أن يأخذه بعينه.
Question: A man bought a cloth and cut it, sewed it, or dyed it, or bought saweeq (parched barley) and mixed it with ghee - i.e., mixed it - then discovered a defect that existed with the seller. What is the ruling? Answer: He claims compensation for the reduction, and the seller may not take it back exactly (as is).
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This ruling covers cases where the buyer alters the item so that it can no longer be returned in its original condition. Examples include cutting and sewing a cloth, dyeing it, or mixing saweeq (a type of food) with ghee. Since the buyer has substantially changed the item, the right of return (khiyar al-ayb) is lost. He may only claim the reduction in value (naqsan) due to the seller's defect. Furthermore, the seller cannot force the buyer to return the altered item even if he wants to; the remedy is limited to financial compensation. This preserves the principle that return requires the item to be in the same condition as delivered.

Key Terms
نقصان (naqsan): Compensation for the reduction in value due to the defect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer buys a cloth, cuts and sews it into a shirt, then discovers a defect, what can he do?
A) Return the shirt to the seller
B) Claim compensation for the defect
C) Demand a replacement cloth
D) Keep the shirt and do nothing

Key Takeaway
When a buyer alters a purchased item, the right of return is lost; only compensation for the defect can be claimed.

Ruling When Sale Item Can No Longer Be Returned
Rules on khiyar al-ayb when return is impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's remedy when a purchased slave is freed or dies before a defect is discovered, and explain why return is replaced by compensation.

Core Idea
If return is impossible, the buyer claims compensation, not the item.

Normally, a buyer with a defective item can return it. But what if the item is no longer in a returnable state? This lesson covers two such cases: the buyer frees the slave or the slave dies. The ruling preserves the buyer's right to compensation while acknowledging the impossibility of return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage gives two situations: (1) the buyer frees the slave after purchase, and (2) the slave dies in the buyer's possession. In both cases, discover a defect that existed with the seller.
2
Determine the remedy
In both scenarios, the buyer does not return the slave; instead, he claims compensation (naqsān) for the price reduction caused by the defect.
The reason given: 'because he is no longer a subject for return and retrieval'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: ما قولكم في من اشترى عبدا وأعتقه، أو مات عنده، ثم اطلع على عيب كان عند البائع ؟ الجواب: رجع بنقصانه ولا يردُّه؛ لأنه لم يبق محلا للرد والاسترداد.
Question: What do you say about a person who bought a slave and freed him, or the slave died in his possession, then discovered a defect that existed with the seller? Answer: He claims compensation for the reduction and does not return him, because he is no longer a subject for return and retrieval.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This ruling covers two situations: (1) the buyer frees the slave after purchase, and (2) the slave dies while with the buyer. In both cases, the item (the slave) is no longer in a state that can be returned to the seller. Therefore, the buyer's right to return is extinguished. However, the buyer still has the right to claim compensation (naqsan) from the seller for the hidden defect. The reasoning given is that the slave is no longer a 'subject for return and retrieval' (لم يبق محلا للرد والاسترداد). This is consistent with the general principle that khiyar al-ayb cannot be exercised if the item is no longer returnable in its original form.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Act of freeing a slave, which transfers ownership from the buyer to the slave.
نقصان (naqsān): Compensation for the reduction in value caused by a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He can rescind the sale and get his money back
B) He can claim compensation for the defect
C) He can demand the seller take back the freed slave
D) He has no remedy

Key Takeaway
When the purchased item can no longer be returned due to death or emancipation, the buyer retains the right to claim compensation for hidden defects.

When Buyer Destroys the Item: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Defect Claims
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation after destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the position of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a buyer's claim after destroying a defective item, and explain the practical outcome (thamarah al-khilaf) of this dispute.

Core Idea
Destroying the item extinguishes return but not necessarily compensation — Sahibayn allow a claim, Abu Hanifa does not.

What happens when a buyer, after purchasing a defective slave or food, destroys it by killing or eating it, and then discovers the defect? Does the buyer have any recourse? This short passage from a Hanafi text reports a known difference of opinion between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how the Hanafi school balances the impossibility of return against the seller's liability for hidden defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The scenario: buyer kills the purchased slave or eats the purchased food, then discovers a defect that existed at the time of sale. The act is complete and the item is no longer returnable.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer has no claim whatsoever: 'He does not claim anything.' The reasoning is that the right to return (radd) is lost, and compensation is tied to return.
His view is stricter on the buyer.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the buyer can claim compensation for the defect (naqsan al-ayb). Their view separates compensation from the ability to return the item.
The source uses 'qala' (they said) to refer to both disciples.
4
Understand the underlying principles
Abu Hanifa treats the buyer's destruction as a waiver of all claims because the return of the item (the primary remedy) is impossible. The Sahibayn see the defect as an independent basis for compensation that survives destruction.
This reflects a broader principle: whether khiyar al-ayb is primarily a right of return or a right to claim reduction.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
Later Hanafi fatwa favors the Sahibayn: the buyer can claim compensation for the defect even after destroying the item. The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the seller pays or not.
In practice, the buyer is not left without remedy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: فإن قتل المشتري العبد، أو كان المبيع طعاما فأكله، ثم اطلع على عيب كان عند البائع، هل يرجع عليه بشيء؟ الجواب: لا يرجع عليه بشيء في قول أبي حنيفة له، وقالا حملا: يرجع بنقصان العيب.
Question: If the buyer killed the slave, or the sold item was food and he ate it, then discovered a defect that existed with the seller, does he claim anything from him? Answer: He does not claim anything from him according to the saying of Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he claims compensation for the defect.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (dispute within the Hanafi school). The scenario: the buyer destroys the item (by killing a slave or eating food) and then discovers a hidden defect. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer has no recourse at all because his own action of destroying the item has extinguished any right to compensation. He cannot return the item and thus cannot claim anything. However, his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that the buyer can still claim compensation (naqsan al-ayb) from the seller for the hidden defect, despite having destroyed the item. This is because the defect was present at the time of sale, and the buyer's right to compensation should not be lost simply due to the impossibility of return. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the later Hanafi school is that of the Sahibayn (the two disciples) in most cases, but the text here reports the raw positions. The difference illustrates the tension between the principle that return is the primary remedy (Abu Hanifa) and the principle that the buyer should be compensated for loss caused by the seller's concealment (Sahibayn).

Key Terms
أبو حنيفة (Abu Hanifa): The founder of the Hanafi school; his position in this case is that the buyer has no claim.
قالا (qala): Refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples.
نقصان العيب (nuqsan al-ayb): The reduction in value caused by the defect; compensation amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a buyer kills a slave and then discovers a defect, what can he claim?
A) The full price
B) Compensation for the defect
C) Nothing
D) A replacement slave

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, the later relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn: a buyer may claim compensation for a hidden defect even after destroying the item.

Returning a Defective Item Through the Chain of Sales: The Role of Qada' al-Qadi
Conditions for returning to original seller after resale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a first buyer who resold an item may return it to the original seller, and explain why a qadi's decree is required.

Core Idea
Return to the original seller is permissible only if the return from the sub-buyer was by a qadi's decree.

This ruling addresses a common commercial scenario: a person buys an item (here a slave), then resells it. The sub-buyer discovers a defect and returns the item to the first buyer. Now, can the first buyer pass that return back to the original seller? The Hanafi answer hinges on one key condition: whether the return from the sub-buyer was formalized through a court judgment (qada' al-qadi). Understanding this condition is essential for properly allocating liability in a chain of sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and actions
The scenario involves three parties: original seller (A), first buyer (B), and second buyer (C). A sells to B, B resells to C. C discovers a defect and returns the item to B. The question is whether B can then return it to A.
2
State the ruling for each case
The text gives two cases: (1) If C's return to B was by a qadi's decree, B may return to A. (2) If C's return was without a qadi's decree, B may not return to A.
3
Identify the operative condition
The decisive factor is the manner of C's return: it must be 'bi qada' al-qadi' (by judgment of the qadi). A private voluntary return does not create a binding right for B to pass the liability upstream.
4
Explain the legal rationale
The qadi's decree ensures that the defect return is legally valid and incontestable. Without it, the return may be cooperative or non-binding, so A's liability cannot be triggered. This protects the original seller from arbitrary returns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: باع عبدا فباعه المشتري ثم رُدَّ على المشتري بعيب هل يجوز له أن يرده على البائع الأول؟ الجواب: إن قبله البائع الثاني - وهو المشتري الأول - بقضاء القاضي، فله أن يرده على بائعه، وإن قبله بغير قضاء القاضي فليس له ذلك.
Question: A person sold a slave, then the buyer (of that slave) sold him, then the slave was returned to the (first) buyer due to a defect. Is it permissible for him to return him to the original seller? Answer: If the second seller (i.e., the first buyer) accepted him by a qadi's decree, then he may return him to his seller; but if he accepted him without a qadi's decree, then he may not do that.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This ruling deals with a chain of sales. A person (original seller) sells a slave to a buyer. That buyer then resells the slave to a third party. If the third party discovers a defect and returns the slave to the (first) buyer, the question is whether that buyer can in turn return the slave to the original seller. The answer depends on how the return from the third party was effected. If the return was by a qadi's decree (court judgment), then it is considered a valid return, and the first buyer can pass on the liability to the original seller. However, if the third party returned the slave voluntarily without a court order, the first buyer cannot force the original seller to accept the return, because the return may not be legally binding. This distinction ensures that return rights are exercised only when properly established through judicial process.

Key Terms
قضاء القاضي (qada' al-qadi): A judicial decree or court judgment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can the first buyer return the slave to the original seller after it was returned to him by the sub-buyer due to a defect?
A) Automatically in all cases
B) Only if the return was by a qadi's decree
C) Only if the second buyer agrees
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, a first buyer who resold and then accepted a return can only reclaim from the original seller if the return was effectuated by a qadi's decree.

Effect of Seller's Immunity from Defects
Ruling when seller declares immunity from all defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of a seller's general disclaimer on the buyer's right to return for defects and identify the Hanafi position on its validity.

Core Idea
A seller's declaration of freedom from all defects forfeits the buyer's right to return irrespective of the defect.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in sales contracts: the seller declares he is not responsible for any defects. The Hanafi school rules this declaration valid, effectively waiving the buyer's right to return the item. Understanding this ruling is crucial for both buyers and sellers in structuring sales terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contractual term
Note the seller's statement: 'I am free from every defect.' This is a general disclaimer (bara'ah 'amma).
The statement must be explicit and unconditional.
2
Recall the default buyer's right
Normally, the buyer has khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) to return a defective item. This default is overridden by the disclaimer.
3
Apply the disclaimer to the ruling
Because the seller disclaimed all defects, the buyer loses the right to return, even if the defect was unknown to both parties.
The ruling is based on the buyer's acceptance of risk.
4
Consider the limits: seller's knowledge
Some later Hanafi scholars allow return if the seller knew of a specific defect and concealed it, but the text gives an absolute rule.
This nuance is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The operative position for fatwa is that the buyer cannot return the item based on the disclaimer alone.
Fraud or concealment by the seller changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: رجل اشترى عبدا، وقال البائع : إني بريء من كل عيب، ثم اطلع المشتري على عيب، هل له أن يرد المبيع المعيب؟ الجواب: ليس له حينئذ أن يردّ بعيب وإن لم يسم البائع جملة العيوب ولم يعدُّها.
Question: A man bought a slave, and the seller said: 'I am free from every defect.' Then the buyer discovered a defect. Does he have the right to return the defective sold item? Answer: He does not then have the right to return due to a defect, even if the seller did not specify the list of defects and did not enumerate them.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This ruling addresses the effect of a general disclaimer (bara'ah) by the seller. If the seller declares that he is not responsible for any defects (e.g., 'sold as is'), the buyer loses the right to return the item for any defect, even if the seller did not list the defects individually. The Hanafi school enforces such disclaimers as a valid contractual term, provided it does not involve fraud or concealment of known defects. The reasoning is that the buyer has agreed to take the risk. However, if the seller actually knew of a specific defect and concealed it, some later Hanafi scholars allow return despite the disclaimer, but the text here is strict: the buyer cannot return. This highlights the importance of clear contractual terms in sales.

Key Terms
بريء من كل عيب (bari' min kulli 'ayb): A statement by the seller disclaiming liability for any defects

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller says 'I am free from every defect', and a defect is later found, can the buyer return the item?
A) Yes, because the seller is liable
B) No, because the disclaimer waives the right
C) Yes, but only if the defect is major
D) Only if the seller knew about the defect

Key Takeaway
A seller's unconditional disclaimer of defects extinguishes the buyer's right to return the item because the buyer gave an implied consent to bear the risk.

Prohibition of Hiding Defects and Cheating
States the ruling that hiding defects is haram, with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on hiding defects in sales, cite the dalil from the hadith, and explain why it is haram according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Concealing defects in a sale is haram and voids the buyer's trust.

This lesson concludes our discussion on khiyar al-ayb. The text makes a clear moral and legal declaration: any form of fraud or concealment of defects is strictly forbidden. The proof comes from a direct action of the Prophet (peace be upon him), showing that hiding defects is not just discouraged but a major sin. Understanding this ruling helps you apply Islamic ethics in commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text explicitly states that 'ghishsh' (cheating) and 'kitman al-ayb' (concealing defects) are haram. Note the exact Arabic phrase.
The act of hiding a defect is the focus.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Sahih Muslim. Recognize that the hadith is direct evidence (dalil naqli) for the ruling.
The hadith is ahad, so its thubut is zanni, but its dalala is qati.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet inserted his hand into the heap and found hidden wetness. This shows that the seller attempted to conceal a defect. The legal link: the Prophet's disapproval implies prohibition.
The words 'فنالت أصابعه' indicate that he felt the moisture, exposing the concealment.
4
Note the consensus
The text implies this ruling is agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) among all schools, as indicated in the analytical toolbox.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغش وكتمان العيب في السلعة حرام. تنسه حرام روی مسلم عن أبي هريرة الله أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مر على صبرة طعام فأدخل يده فيها، فنالت أصابعه
Cheating and concealing defects in the commodity are forbidden (haram). Note: It is haram. Narrated by Muslim from Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) passed by a heap of food, inserted his hand into it, and his fingers touched [something wet].
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This final passage is a moral and legal conclusion to the discussion on khiyar al-ayb. It declares that any form of fraud (ghishsh) and concealment of defects is strictly prohibited (haram) in Islamic law. The author cites a well-known hadith from Sahih Muslim: The Prophet (peace be upon him) once passed by a food seller, put his hand into the heap, and found it wet underneath, indicating that the seller had hidden a defect (moisture). This hadith is used as evidence that concealing defects is a major sin. The Hanafi school takes this prohibition seriously; it not only affects the validity of the sale (giving the buyer the right of return) but also carries moral consequences for the seller. The word 'تنسه' (tansah) is likely a note for the student to memorize this ruling.

Key Terms
الغش (al-ghishsh): Cheating or fraud in transactions
كتمان العيب (kitman al-ayb): Concealing a defect from the buyer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on hiding defects in a sale according to the passage?
A) Makruh (disliked)
B) Mubah (permissible if disclosed later)
C) Haram (forbidden)
D) Wajib (obligatory) to disclose

Key Takeaway
Concealing defects in sales is absolutely forbidden (haram) and gives the buyer the right to return the item.

12 min
Rulings on the Option of Defect (Khiyar al-'Ayb) in Hanafi Fiqh
Question-answer rulings on defects in sales with a hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Khiyar al-'Ayb for various scenarios (alteration, destruction, disclaimer) and identify the relied-upon position in the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
The buyer may claim compensation for a pre-existing defect even after alteration, but destruction triggers a dispute: mufta bihi is compensation.

This lesson presents a series of question-answer rulings on the option of defect, covering key scenarios where the buyer discovers a defect after changing or consuming the item. The focus is on the remedy: compensation (naqsan) rather than return. The lesson also addresses a special disclaimer by the seller and the fundamental prohibition of cheating, supported by hadith. You will learn the default rule, the intra-Hanafi dispute on destruction, and the effect of a general disclaimer.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the default remedy
Identify the default ruling: when a pre-existing defect is discovered, the buyer may claim compensation (naqsan al-'ayb) but does not return the item unless the seller agrees. This is the basic principle.
The remedy is compensation, not return, in Hanafi law.
2
Apply to alteration and consumption cases
Examine cases where the buyer has altered the item (cut/sewed cloth, mixed sawiq) or it has perished (slave died, buyer freed slave). In all these, the buyer still receives compensation because the defect existed prior, and the item is no longer returnable.
Alteration does not eliminate the right to compensation.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on destruction
Focus on the case where the buyer destroys the item (kills a slave, eats food). Abu Hanifa says no compensation; the two disciples (Sahibayn) say compensation is due. Understand the illa: Abu Hanifa sees the destruction as superseding the defect, while Sahibayn see the defect as independent.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Understand the effect of a general disclaimer
If the seller says 'I am free from every defect' without enumerating, the buyer loses the right to return for any undisclosed defect. This is a form of waiver, but only effective if the buyer knows the defect or the disclaimer is specific.
General disclaimers are insufficient to absolve the seller of liability for unknown defects.
5
Connect to the hadith proof
The passage ends with a hadith from Muslim and Ibn Majah explicitly prohibiting cheating and concealing defects. Identify the basis: the Prophet's rhetorical question and his statement 'Whoever cheats is not of me' make the act haram. This serves as the dalil for the entire doctrine.
The hadith establishes the ethical foundation: concealment of defects is a major sin.
