خيار العيب
الجواب له أن يرجع بنقصان العيب، ولا يرد المبيع إلا أن يرضى البائع أن يأخذه بعيبه الذي حدث عند المشتري.
السؤال اشترى ثوبا وقطعه وخاطه أو صبغه، أو اشترى سويقا فلته بسمن -أي خلطه به - ثم اطلع على عيب كان عند البائع، ما حكمه؟
الجواب: رجع بنقصانه، وليس للبائع أن يأخذه بعينه. السؤال: ما قولكم في من اشترى عبدا وأعتقه، أو مات عنده، ثم اطلع على عيب كان عند البائع ؟
الجواب: رجع بنقصانه ولا يردُّه؛ لأنه لم يبق محلا للرد والاسترداد.
السؤال: فإن قتل المشتري العبد، أو كان المبيع طعاما فأكله، ثم اطلع على عيب كان عند البائع، هل يرجع عليه بشيء؟
الجواب: لا يرجع عليه بشيء في قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، وقالا هما: يرجع بنقصان العيب. السؤال: باع عبدا فباعه المشتري ثم رُدَّ على المشتري بعيب هل يجوز له أن يرده على البائع الأول؟
الجواب: إن قبله البائع الثاني - وهو المشتري الأول - بقضاء القاضي، فله أن يرده على بائعه، وإن قبله بغير قضاء القاضي فليس له ذلك.
السؤال: رجل اشترى عبدا، وقال البائع : إني بريء من كل عيب، ثم اطلع المشتري على عيب، هل له أن يرد المبيع المعيب؟
الجواب: ليس له حينئذ أن يردّ بعيب وإن لم يسم البائع جملة العيوب ولم يعدُّها.
الغش وكتمان العيب في السلعة حرام.
[؟تنسه] حرام روی مسلم عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مر على صبرة طعام فأدخل يده فيها، فنالت أصابعه بللا، فقال: ما هذا يا صاحب الطعام؟ قال : أصابته السماء يا رسول الله ! قال: "أفلا جعلته فوق الطعام حتى يراه الناس، من غش فليس مني." وروى ابن ماجه عن وائلة بن الأسقع رضي الله عنه قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: من باع عيبا لم يبينه لم يزل في مقت الله، أو قال: لم تزل الملائكة تلعنه.
Chapter: Option of Defect
Answer: He may claim compensation for the defect, and he does not return the sold item unless the seller agrees to take it with the defect that occurred with the buyer.
Question: He bought a cloth and cut it and sewed it, or dyed it, or bought sawiq and mixed it with ghee - i.e. mixed it with it - then discovered a defect that existed with the seller, what is the ruling?
Answer: He claims compensation for the depreciation, and the seller cannot take it back in kind.
Question: What is your opinion about one who bought a slave and freed him, or the slave died with him, then discovered a defect that existed with the seller?
Answer: He claims compensation for the depreciation and does not return him; because he is no longer subject to return and retrieval.
Question: If the buyer killed the slave, or the sold item was food and he ate it, then discovered a defect that existed with the seller, does he claim anything from him?
Answer: He does not claim anything according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He claims compensation for the defect.
Question: He sold a slave, then the buyer sold him, then the slave was returned to the (first) buyer due to a defect, is it permissible for him to return him to the original seller?
Answer: If the second seller - who is the first buyer - accepted him by a court order, then he may return him to his (own) seller; if he accepted him without a court order, then he does not have that right.
Question: A man bought a slave, and the seller said: I am free from every defect, then the buyer discovered a defect, does he have the right to return the defective sold item?
Answer: He does not have the right to return due to a defect then, even if the seller did not specify the list of defects and did not enumerate them.
Cheating and concealing a defect in the commodity is forbidden.
[?Note] Forbidden. Narrated by Muslim from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, passed by a heap of food and inserted his hand into it, and his fingers got wet. He said: 'What is this, O owner of the food?' He said: 'It was rained upon, O Messenger of Allah.' He said: 'Why did you not put it on top of the food so that people could see it? Whoever cheats is not of me.' And Ibn Majah narrated from Wathila ibn al-Asqa', may Allah be pleased with him, who said: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: 'Whoever sells a defect and does not disclose it will continue to be in Allah's wrath' - or he said: 'The angels will continue to curse him.'
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) in Hanafi fiqh, in a question-answer format. The key principle is that when a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect after sale, he has the right to claim compensation for the depreciation in value (naqsan al-'ayb) without returning the item, unless the seller agrees to take it back. This applies even if the buyer has altered the item (e.g., cut and sewed cloth, mixed sawiq with ghee) or if the buyer has freed or the slave has died. However, if the buyer destroys the item (e.g., kills the slave, eats the food), there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that no compensation is due, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that compensation is still payable. The relied-upon position in the later school is that of the disciples. Another scenario: if the buyer sells the item to a third party and it is returned to him due to a defect, he may return it to his own seller only if the acceptance from the third party was by court order; otherwise not. If the seller explicitly disclaims all defects without enumerating them, the buyer loses the right to return for any undisclosed defect. The passage concludes with a hadith from Muslim and Ibn Majah firmly establishing that cheating and concealing defects in sales is haram, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) expressed severe disapproval. The hadith serves as the primary proof for the prohibition of fraud in transactions.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option available to the buyer upon discovering a pre-existing defect in the purchased item, allowing him to claim compensation or return the item under certain conditions.
نقصان العيب (naqsan al-'ayb): The difference in value between the sound item and the defective item at the time of sale, which the buyer can claim from the seller.
غش (ghish): Cheating or fraud in a transaction, including concealing defects, which is strictly prohibited.
بريء من كل عيب (bari' min kulli 'ayb): A seller's declaration that he is free from responsibility for any defect, which in Hanafi law does not absolve him if the defect is not specifically enumerated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases a slave, kills him, and then discovers a pre-existing defect, what is the ruling according to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa?
A) The buyer can return the dead slave and get a full refund.
B) The buyer can claim compensation for the defect from the seller.
C) The buyer has no recourse at all because he destroyed the item.
D) The buyer can claim compensation only if the seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the buyer compensation for a pre-existing defect even if the item has been altered or destroyed, based on the relied-upon view that the seller's liability is independent of the buyer's subsequent actions, and cheating in sales is strictly prohibited by hadith.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Classification of Sales: Valid, Void, Invalid, and Disliked
Defining and distinguishing categories of sales in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four categories of sales (sahih, batil, fasid, makruh tahriman) in Hanafi fiqh and state the legal consequence of each.

Core Idea
A fasid sale can become valid through possession; a batil sale never transfers ownership.

Sales are not simply valid or invalid in Hanafi fiqh. The school distinguishes between categories based on whether the contract is sound in its essence (subject matter) and description (terms, conditions). Understanding these distinctions is essential for determining ownership transfer and legal obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List the four divisions of sale: sahih, batil, fasid, and makruh tahriman. Note that the passage also subdivides permissible sales into three types.
These categories are based on the ruling of the sale itself, not external factors.
2
Understand 'essence' vs 'description'
Essence (asl) refers to the subject matter being valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). Description (wasf) refers to the terms, conditions, and absence of prohibitions. A sale can be valid in one but not the other.
A fasid sale is valid in essence but flawed in description.
3
Determine the legal effect of each category
Sahih: immediate ownership. Batil: no ownership ever. Fasid: ownership if possession (qabd) is taken with seller's permission. Makruh tahriman: valid but sinful.
The fasid category is unique to the Hanafi school.
4
Distinguish fasid from batil
Fasid can become valid through possession; batil cannot. Batil is completely unlawful in essence and description.
Jurists sometimes use 'fasid' loosely to include batil, but the technical distinction matters for rulings.
5
Recognize makruh tahriman
A sale that is valid but prohibited for an external reason, such as selling during Friday adhan. It does not affect the contract's validity.
This is different from fasid, which has an internal defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البيع الفاسد والباطل والمكروه
السؤال: بينوا أقسام البيع من حيث الصحة والفساد.
الجواب: البيع على أربعة أقسام:
١- صحيح: وهو ما كان مشروعا بأصله ووصفه، وهو يفيد الحكم بنفسه إذا خلا عن الموانع.
٢ - باطل: وهو ما كان غير مشروع أصلا، ولا يفيد الحكم بأي حال.
٣- فاسد: وهو ما كان مشروعا بأصله دون وصفه، وهو يفيد الحكم إذا اتصل به قبض المشتري، وقد يتسامح الفقهاء فيطلقون اسم الفاسد على الباطل.
أربعة أقسام: قال في البحر الرائق (٧٥/٦) والبياعات المنهي عنها ثلاثة : فاسد وباطل ومكروه تحريما، فالفاسد بيناه وهو ما كان مشروعا بأصله لا بوصفه ومرادهم من مشروعية أصله كونه مالا متقوما: لا جوازه وصحته؛ فإن كونه فاسدا يمنع صحته. وأما الثاني فهو ما لا يكون مشروعا لا بأصله ولا بوصفه، وحكمه عدم إفادة الحكم وهو الملك، قبضه أم لا. وأما المكروه: فهو لغةً خلاف المحبوب، واصطلاحاً ما نُهِيَ عنه المجاور، كالبيع عند أذان الجمعة، نهي عنه للصلاة، ويمكن إدخاله تحت الفاسد أيضا على إرادة الأعم وهو ما نُهي عنه فيشمل الثلاثة، والفساد بالمعنى الأعم يثبت بأسباب منها الجهالة المفضية إلى المنازعة في المبيع أو الثمن، ومنها العجز من التسليم إلا بضرر، منها الغرر، ومنها شرط خارج عن الشرع، ومنها عدم المالية أو التقوم ومنها عدم الوجود، ومنها عدم القدرة على التسليم.
وأما البيع الجائز الذي لا نَهْي فيه فثلاثة نافذ لازم ونافذ ليس بلازم وموقوف، فالأول: ما كان مشروعا بأصله ووصفه، ولم يتعلق به حق الغير ولا خيار فيه والثاني ما لم يتعلق به حق الغير وفيه خيار. والموقوف ما تعلق به حق الغير، وهو إما ملك الغير أو حق بالمبيع لغير المالك. (انتهى ملخصا)
فيطلقون: كما فعل العلامة القدوري رحمه الله، فإنه عقد باب البيع الفاسد وذكر في ذيله البيع الباطل أيضا.
The Invalid (Fasid), Void (Batil), and Disliked (Makruh) Sale
Question: Explain the divisions of sale in terms of validity and invalidity.
Answer: Sale is of four divisions:
1. Valid (Sahih): That which is legislated in its essence and description, and it produces legal effect by itself when free from impediments.
2. Void (Batil): That which is not legislated at all, and does not produce legal effect in any case.
3. Invalid (Fasid): That which is legislated in its essence but not in its description, and it produces legal effect if possession (qabd) by the buyer is connected to it, and jurists sometimes tolerate calling fasid as batil.
Four divisions: It is said in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (6/75): The forbidden sales are three: fasid, batil, and makruh tahriman. As for fasid, we have explained it: it is that which is legislated in its essence but not in its description, and their intent by 'legislated in its essence' is that it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), not its permissibility and validity; for its being fasid prevents its validity. As for the second (batil), it is that which is not legislated in its essence nor in its description, and its ruling is that it does not produce legal effect, which is ownership, whether possession is taken or not. As for makruh (disliked): It is linguistically the opposite of beloved, and technically that which is prohibited due to a peripheral (adjacent) matter, like the sale at the time of the Friday adhan, prohibited for the prayer, and it can also be included under fasid in the general sense, taking 'what is prohibited' to include all three. Invalidity in the general sense is established by causes: ignorance leading to dispute over the sold item or price, inability to deliver except with harm, gharar (uncertainty), a condition outside the Shari'a, lack of monetary value or being a valuable property (taqawwum), non-existence, and inability to deliver.
As for the permissible (ja'iz) sale in which there is no prohibition, there are three: executable and binding (nafidh lazim), executable but not binding (nafidh ghayr lazim), and suspended (mawquf). The first is that which is legislated in its essence and description, and no right of a third party is attached to it, and there is no option in it. The second is that in which no right of a third party is attached, but there is an option in it. The suspended is that to which a right of a third party is attached, whether the ownership of another or a right in the sold item belonging to a non-owner. (End, summarized)
They sometimes use (fasid for batil): as did the scholar al-Quduri, may Allah have mercy on him, for he titled the chapter of fasid sale and mentioned batil sale also in its appendage.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage provides a systematic classification of sales in Hanafi fiqh according to their validity. The primary categories are: Sahih (valid), Batil (void), Fasid (invalid), and Makruh Tahriman (prohibitively disliked). A valid sale (sahih) is one that is fully legislated in both its essence and description, and it produces legal effects (transfer of ownership) immediately. A void sale (batil) is one that lacks legitimacy in both essence and description, such as selling something that is not property, and it never produces ownership. An invalid sale (fasid) is one that is legitimate in essence (the object is valuable property) but flawed in description (e.g., due to uncertainty). The fasid sale can become effective if the buyer takes possession (qabd) with the seller's permission. There is also a fourth category: makruh tahriman, which is prohibited for a reason external to the contract, like selling during the Friday adhan. The passage clarifies that jurists sometimes use 'fasid' broadly to include 'batil'. It also lists causes of fasād in the general sense: ignorance, inability to deliver, gharar, invalid conditions, etc. Additionally, permissible sales (ja'iz) are divided into three: nafidh lazim (executory and binding, no options), nafidh ghayr lazim (executory but revocable due to options), and mawquf (suspended on third-party consent). The author notes that al-Quduri used 'fasid' to also cover 'batil' in his chapter. This classification is fundamental to Hanafi contract law.

Key Terms
صحيح (sahih): A sale that is lawful in both essence (subject matter) and description (price, consent, etc.) and produces immediate legal effects.
باطل (batil): A sale that lacks both essential and descriptive validity, such as selling forbidden items, and produces no ownership at all.
فاسد (fasid): A sale that is valid in essence (the item is property) but defective in description (e.g., due to gharar or ignorance), which can become effective if the buyer takes possession.
مكروه تحريما (makruh tahriman): A sale that is valid but prohibited for an external reason, like selling during Friday adhan; it is sinful but the contract is not invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a fasid sale from a batil sale in Hanafi fiqh?
A) A fasid sale is prohibited for an external reason, while batil is prohibited intrinsically.
B) A fasid sale can become effective if the buyer takes possession, while batil never produces ownership.
C) A fasid sale is valid in both essence and description, while batil is valid in neither.
D) A fasid sale is always invalid, while batil can be validated by a court order.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely recognizes the fasid sale, which can become valid through possession, distinguishing it from the irredeemable batil sale.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Classification of Sales: Sahih, Batil, Fasid, and Makruh
Defines and contrasts valid, void, defective, and disliked sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi categories of sales and explain the unique distinguishing feature of a fasid sale: it becomes valid after possession.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a fasid sale is not void ab initio but can become valid after possession – a position unique among the schools.

This passage from a Hanafi manual defines the core classification of sales: valid (sahih), void (batil), invalid (fasid), and disliked (makruh). These categories determine when ownership transfers legally. The most distinctive Hanafi concept is the fasid sale, which is defective in description but can become valid once the buyer takes possession. Understanding this gradation is essential for correctly analysing contracts in Islamic commercial law.

Guided Path
1
List the four primary divisions
Identify the four categories: sahih, batil, fasid, and makruh. Note that the text also introduces ja'iz (permissible) after the four.
2
Define 'legislated by essence' and 'legislated by description'
Essence means the subject is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). Description refers to the proper form of the contract, including clarity of terms and absence of defects. A sale is sahih only when both are present.
3
Grasp the Hanafi concept of fasid
A fasid sale is legislated by essence (it concerns valuable property) but defective in description (e.g., unknown price). Unlike batil, it can become effective – but only after the buyer takes possession (qabd). This is a distinctive Hanafi position that other schools reject.
4
Distinguish batil from fasid
Batil lacks validity in both essence and description; it never transfers ownership, even after possession. Fasid lacks only in description and can become valid after possession.
5
Explain makruh sales and the categories of ja'iz
Makruh sales are prohibited but not severely, like selling during Friday prayer. They can be subsumed under a broader meaning of fasid. Permissible sales (ja'iz) are further divided into binding, non‑binding (with option), and suspended (dependent on third‑party ratification).

Source Text

كتاب البيوع
البيع الفاسد والباطل والمكروه
السؤال: بينوا أقسام البيع من حيث الصحة والفساد.
الجواب: البيع على أربعة أقسام:
١- صحيح: وهو ما كان مشروعا بأصله ووصفه، وهو يفيد الحكم بنفسه إذا خلا عن الموانع.
٢ - باطل: وهو ما كان غير مشروع أصلا، ولا يفيد الحكم بأي حال.
٣- فاسد: وهو ما كان مشروعا بأصله دون وصفه، وهو يفيد الحكم إذا اتصل به قبض المشتري، وقد يتسامح الفقهاء فيطلقون اسم الفاسد على الباطل.
بللا، فقال: ما هذا يا صاحب الطعام؟ قال : أصابته السماء يا رسول الله ! قال: "أفلا جعلته فوق الطعام حتى يراه الناس، من غش فليس مني." وروى ابن ماجه عن وائلة بن الأسقع رضي الله عنه قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: "من باع عيبا لم يبينه لم يزل في مقت الله"، أو قال: "لم تزل الملائكة تلعنه."

وأما البيع الجائز الذي لا نَهْي فيه فثلاثة نافذ لازم ونافذ ليس بلازم وموقوف، فالأول: ما كان مشروعا بأصله ووصفه، ولم يتعلق به حق الغير ولا خيار فيه والثاني ما لم يتعلق به حق الغير وفيه خيار. والموقوف ما تعلق به حق الغير، وهو إما ملك الغير أو حق بالمبيع لغير المالك. (انتهى ملخصا)
فيطلقون: كما فعل العلامة القدوري رحمه الله، فإنه عقد باب البيع الفاسد وذكر في ذيله البيع الباطل أيضا.
Book of Sales
Invalid, Void, and Disliked Sale
Question: Explain the divisions of sale with respect to validity and invalidity.
Answer: Sale is of four divisions:
1- Valid: It is that which is legislated in its essence and description, and it effects the ruling by itself if free from impediments.
2- Void: It is that which is not legislated at all in its essence, and it does not effect the ruling in any case.
3- Invalid: It is that which is legislated in its essence but not in its description, and it effects the ruling if possession of the buyer is connected to it. The jurists sometimes tolerate and call void by the name of invalid.
[Then a hadith about cheating:] "[...] He said: 'What is this, O owner of the food?' He said: 'It was rained upon, O Messenger of Allah!' He said: 'Why did you not put it on top of the food so that people could see it? Whoever cheats is not of me.'" And Ibn Majah narrated from Wa'ilah ibn al-Asqa' that he said: I heard the Messenger of Allah say: "Whoever sells a defect without disclosing it will continue to be in Allah's wrath," or he said: "The angels will continue to curse him."
[Then a quote from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq:] Four divisions: He said in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (6/75): The prohibited sales are three: invalid, void, and prohibitively disliked. The invalid we have explained, and it is that which is legislated in its essence but not in its description, and their intention by 'legislated in its essence' is that it is valuable property, not its permissibility and validity; for its being invalid prevents its validity. As for the second, it is that which is not legislated in its essence nor in its description, and its ruling is that it does not effect the ruling, which is ownership, whether possession is taken or not. As for the disliked (makruh): linguistically it is the opposite of beloved, and terminologically it is that which is prohibited but not severely, like sale at the time of the Friday call to prayer, prohibited because of prayer. It can also be included under invalid in the general sense, which is that which is prohibited, and it includes the three. Invalidity in the general sense is established by causes: ignorance leading to dispute in the sold item or price, inability to deliver except with harm, uncertainty, a condition outside the Sharia, lack of financial value or valuable property, non-existence, and inability to deliver.
As for the permissible sale in which there is no prohibition, it is of three types: effective and binding, effective but not binding, and suspended. The first: that which is legislated in its essence and description, and no right of another is attached to it, nor is there any option in it. The second: that which no right of another is attached to it, and there is an option in it. The suspended: that which a right of another is attached to it, and it is either the property of another or a right in the sold item belonging to someone other than the owner. (End of summary)
They tolerate: as did the scholar al-Quduri, for he wrote a chapter on invalid sale and mentioned void sale in its appendix.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of sales in Hanafi law. The author begins by presenting four basic types: valid (sahih), void (batil), invalid (fasid), and then adds a discussion of disliked (makruh) and permissible (ja'iz) categories. The valid sale is flawless in both essence and description, and it immediately transfers ownership. The void sale is fundamentally unlegislated and never transfers ownership, even after possession. The invalid (fasid) sale is defective in description but can become valid after possession by the buyer; this is a distinctively Hanafi position. Then two hadiths are cited as proof for the prohibition of dishonesty in sales. Next, a longer quote from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refines the categories: prohibited sales are of three types—fasid, batil, and makruh tahriman. Fasid is defined as having valuable property as its essence but lacking a proper description; batil lacks both; makruh is a lesser prohibition like selling during the Friday prayer. The general concept of fasid is then expanded to include any prohibited sale (including batil and makruh) due to causes like ignorance (jahalah), inability to deliver, uncertainty (gharar), unlawful conditions, non-item nature, or non-existence. Finally, permissible sales are divided into three: binding (nashid) with no option or third-party rights, non-binding with options, and suspended (mawquf) when a third party has a right that may be ratified. This classification is foundational for Hanafi sales law.

Key Terms
البيع الصحيح (al-bay' al-sahih): A sale legislated in its essence and description, free from impediments; it transfers ownership immediately.
البيع الباطل (al-bay' al-batil): A sale not legislated in its essence at all; it never transfers ownership, even after possession.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale legislated in essence (valuable property) but defective in description; it becomes valid and transfers ownership only after the buyer takes possession.
البيع المكروه (al-bay' al-makruh): A sale that is prohibited but not severely, such as sale during the Friday call to prayer; it is not invalid but discouraged.
البيع الجائز (al-bay' al-ja'iz): A permissible sale that is not prohibited; it includes three subcategories: nafidh lazim (binding), nafidh ghayr lazim (with option), and mawquf (suspended).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, what happens to an invalid (fasid) sale after the buyer takes possession of the item?
A) It remains void and ownership does not transfer.
B) It becomes valid and ownership transfers.
C) It becomes valid only if the seller ratifies it.
D) It becomes valid but the buyer must pay double the price.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a fasid sale is not hopelessly void – it can become perfectly valid after the buyer takes possession, a ruling that has no parallel in the other Sunni schools.

beginner
5 min
Definition of Batil Sale (Bay' Batil)
Definition of batil sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions that render a sale batil and apply them to any given scenario.

Core Idea
A sale is batil if its price or subject matter is unlawful or not owned.

This lesson examines the foundational definition of an invalid sale (bay' batil) from the Hanafi school. Understanding this definition is crucial because it distinguishes batil from fasid and provides a clear criterion to evaluate any transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The definition states that a sale is batil if either the price (thaman) or the object (muthman) is haram (unlawful) or not owned (ghayr mamluk).
Note that 'non-owned' includes things not considered property at all, like carrion or blood.
2
Apply to simple examples
Test the definition on cases: selling wine (haram subject), selling a stolen car (non-owned). Both are batil.
3
Contrast with fasid
In Hanafi fiqh, batil is void ab initio with no legal effect, while fasid is irregular but potentially curable by removing the defect.
Example: selling with an unspecified price is fasid if it can be rectified; selling a dead animal is batil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة البيع الباطل
٤ - موقوف : كما إذا باع ملك الغير وهو يفيد الحكم على سبيل التوقف، فإن أجاز المالك نفذ، وإلا بطل.
أمثلة البيع الباطل وحكمه
السؤال: بينوا بالإجمال صورة البيع الباطل.
الجواب: إذا كان أحد العوضين أو كلاهما محرما أو كان غير مملوك فالبيع باطل.
السؤال: بينوا بعض أمثلة البيع الباطل.
الجواب: بطل بيع المعدوم وما ليس بمال كالدم المسفوح والميتة، والحر، والمضامين والملاقيح، والحمل، والنتاج، ومتروك التسمية عمدا، وبطل بيع جلد ميتة قبل دباغه، وبيع شاة ذكية ضمت إلى شاة ماتت حتف أنفها، وبيع شعر الإنسان لكرامته، وبيع شعر الخنزير لنجاسته، وبطل بيع رجيع آدمي لم يغلب عليه التراب وبيع ما ليس في ملكه إلا أن يكون بطريق السلم، وبطل بيع سمك لم يصد، وبيع طير في الهواء، وبطل بيع لبن في ضرع، ولؤلؤ في صدف، وصوف على ظهر غنم، وبطل بيع خمر وخنزير، وقن ضُمَّ إلى حر، كما بطل بيع الكلأ في المرعى.
الميتة إلا السمك والجراد الحمل - بسكون الميم - الجنين والنتاج حمل الحبلة، والبيع فيهما باطل، وفي "مصنف عبد الرزاق": نُهي عن المضامين والملاقيح وحبل الحبلة والمضامين جمع مضمونة، ما في أصلاب الإبل، والملاقيح: جمع ملقوح، ما في بطونها، وقيل بالعكس وحبل الحبلة: ولد ولد الناقة، و"في السراج الوهاج": لا يجوز بيع الحمل وحده دون الأم، ولا الأم دونه، فلو باع الحمل وولدت قبل الافتراق وسلم لا يجوز من البحر الرائق (٨٠/٦) التراب فلو مغلوبا جاز كسرقين وبعر، واكتفى في "البحر" بمجرد خلطه بتراب من "الدر المختار". السلم: ستعرف معنى السلم في بابه إن شاء الله تعالى.
المرعى أي لا يجوز بيع الكلأ وإجارته، أما البيع؛ فلأنه ورد على ما لا يملكه لاشتراك الناس فيه بالحديث، وأما الإجارة؛ فلأنها عقدت على استهلاك عين مباح "البحر الرائق" (۸۳/٦) ، وفي "الدر المختار": هذا إذا نبت
Examples of Invalid Sale
4 - Suspended: such as when one sells the property of another; it confers a ruling contingent upon permission. If the owner permits, it is effective; otherwise it is void.
Examples of Invalid Sale and Its Ruling
Question: Briefly describe the scenario of an invalid sale.
Answer: If one of the two exchanges or both are prohibited or are not owned, then the sale is invalid.
Question: Provide some examples of invalid sale.
Answer: Invalid is the sale of a non-existent thing and what is not property, like spilled blood and carrion, and a free person, and mudamin and malaqih, and the fetus (haml), and the offspring (nitaj), and intentionally omitting the name (of Allah), and invalid is the sale of the skin of a carrion before tanning, and selling a slaughtered sheep mixed with a sheep that died naturally, and selling human hair due to its dignity, and selling pig hair due to its impurity, and invalid is the sale of human feces not overcome by soil, and selling what is not in one's possession except by way of salam, and invalid is the sale of fish not yet caught, and a bird in the air, and invalid is the sale of milk in the udder, pearls in the shell, wool on the back of sheep, and invalid is the sale of wine and pig, and a slave attached to a free person, as well as the sale of grass in the pasture.
Carrion except fish and locusts; al-haml (with sukun on the meem) is the fetus, and nitaj is the offspring of the she-camel's pregnancy, and selling them is invalid. In the Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq: prohibition of mudamin and malaqih and habl al-habla. Mudamin: plural of madmuna, what is in the loins of camels; malaqih: plural of maluqih, what is in their bellies; it is also said the opposite. Habl al-habla: the offspring of the she-camel's offspring. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: it is not permissible to sell the fetus alone without the mother, nor the mother without it. If one sells the fetus and she gives birth before separation, it is not permissible. From al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (6/80): soil; if [feces] is overcome [by soil] it is permissible like dung and pellets; in al-Bahr they are content with mere mixing with soil from al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Salam: you will come to know the meaning of salam in its chapter, God willing.
Al-Mar'a: i.e., it is not permissible to sell grass nor to lease it; as for sale, because it occurs on what is not owned due to the common ownership of people as per the hadith; as for lease, because it is contracted on the consumption of a permissible substance. From al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (6/83). In al-Durr al-Mukhtar: this is if it grows.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with accompanying expansion from later commentaries. It begins by defining invalid sale (bay' batil) in general: when either the price, the object, or both are unlawful or not owned. Then it lists numerous specific examples of invalid sales, including: sale of non-existent items (ma'dum), things not considered property (like blood, carrion), free persons, certain types of unborn animals (mudamin, malaqih, habl al-habla), intentionally omitting the basmalah when slaughtering, untanned carrion skin, mixing permissible and impermissible meat, human hair (respected), pig hair (impure), human feces not mixed with earth, sale of what one does not own unless via salam, uncaptured fish, birds in the air, milk in the udder, pearls in shells, wool on sheep, wine, pigs, combining a slave with a free person, and grass in a common pasture. The commentary then explains specific terms: 'carrion' except fish and locusts; 'haml' is the fetus; 'nitaj' is the offspring; hadith from Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq prohibiting mudamin (what is in the loins of camels) and malaqih (what is in the bellies) and habl al-habla (offspring of the offspring). The commentary notes that selling the fetus alone or the mother alone is not permissible. The discussion of feces indicates that if it is mixed with soil it becomes permissible to sell (like fertilizer). The last part addresses grass: selling or leasing grass in a common pasture is invalid because it is owned collectively by all Muslims; only after one cultivates it by watering and tending does it become his property, and then it may be sold. However, there is a difference of opinion: al-Quduri (and some later scholars) hold that ownership is not achieved by watering alone but only by actual possession (hayazah), but the majority of later scholars (mutakhkhirin) permit the sale.

Key Terms
البيع الباطل (al-bay' al-batil): A sale that is null and void from its inception due to a defect in the subject matter or consideration (e.g., non-existent, impure, or not owned).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, which of the following makes a sale batil?
A) The price is too low
B) The subject matter is not owned by the seller
C) The sale is conducted without witnesses
D) The delivery is delayed

Key Takeaway
A sale is batil if either the price or the object is unlawful or not owned, void from the start.

Examples of Batil Sale: Non-Existent, Impure, and Non-Owned Items
List of examples of batil sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify specific cases of batil sale from the Hanafi fiqh list and explain the underlying defect for each.

Core Idea
Batil sales include non-existent items, impure substances, and things not owned or not property.

After grasping the definition, we now examine concrete examples of batil sale as listed by al-Quduri. This lesson will equip you with a practical inventory of transactions that are void ab initio.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the examples
Group the examples into three categories: non-existent (ma'dum), impure (najis), and non-owned (ghayr mamluk).
Some overlap: human hair is respected, not impure, so its sale is batil due to dignity.
2
Identify the specific defect per example
For each item, state whether the defect is in the subject matter (unlawful, non-existent, or not property) or in ownership.
Example: milk in udder is non-existent at sale; wine is impure.
3
Apply the rule to similar cases
Use the examples to deduce that selling something uncertain (gharar) or non-existent is batil, except salam.
Salam is an exception for future goods with precise description.

Sale of Grass (Kala') and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explanation of the grass example and the ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the sale of common pasture grass is batil and state the later Hanafi position regarding cultivated grass.

Core Idea
Grass in a common pasture is owned by all, so its sale is batil, but watering and tending it can make it ownable.

Among the examples of batil sale, grass growing in a common pasture (kala' fi al-mar'a) is highlighted. This lesson delves into the reasoning behind its invalidity and the nuanced intra-Hanafi difference of opinion regarding grass that one has cultivated.

Guided Path
1
Understand why grass is initially not ownable
Grass in a common pasture is considered mushā' (common property) owned by all Muslims collectively, so an individual cannot sell it.
Hadith: 'People are partners in three: water, pasture, and fire.'
2
Learn the effect of cultivation
If a person waters and tends the grass, it becomes his property through work, and later Hanafi scholars consider its sale valid.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Quduri held that mere watering does not grant ownership; possession (hayazah) is required. The majority (including Marghinani and later fatwa) permit sale after cultivation.
The mufta bihi position is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة البيع الباطل
٤ - موقوف : كما إذا باع ملك الغير وهو يفيد الحكم على سبيل التوقف، فإن أجاز المالك نفذ، وإلا بطل.
أمثلة البيع الباطل وحكمه
السؤال: بينوا بالإجمال صورة البيع الباطل.
الجواب: إذا كان أحد العوضين أو كلاهما محرما أو كان غير مملوك فالبيع باطل.
السؤال: بينوا بعض أمثلة البيع الباطل.
الجواب: بطل بيع المعدوم وما ليس بمال كالدم المسفوح والميتة، والحر، والمضامين والملاقيح، والحمل، والنتاج، ومتروك التسمية عمدا، وبطل بيع جلد ميتة قبل دباغه، وبيع شاة ذكية ضمت إلى شاة ماتت حتف أنفها، وبيع شعر الإنسان لكرامته، وبيع شعر الخنزير لنجاسته، وبطل بيع رجيع آدمي لم يغلب عليه التراب وبيع ما ليس في ملكه إلا أن يكون بطريق السلم، وبطل بيع سمك لم يصد، وبيع طير في الهواء، وبطل بيع لبن في ضرع، ولؤلؤ في صدف، وصوف على ظهر غنم، وبطل بيع خمر وخنزير، وقن ضُمَّ إلى حر، كما بطل بيع الكلأ في المرعى.
الميتة إلا السمك والجراد الحمل - بسكون الميم - الجنين والنتاج حمل الحبلة، والبيع فيهما باطل، وفي "مصنف عبد الرزاق": نُهي عن المضامين والملاقيح وحبل الحبلة والمضامين جمع مضمونة، ما في أصلاب الإبل، والملاقيح: جمع ملقوح، ما في بطونها، وقيل بالعكس وحبل الحبلة: ولد ولد الناقة، و"في السراج الوهاج": لا يجوز بيع الحمل وحده دون الأم، ولا الأم دونه، فلو باع الحمل وولدت قبل الافتراق وسلم لا يجوز من البحر الرائق (٨٠/٦) التراب فلو مغلوبا جاز كسرقين وبعر، واكتفى في "البحر" بمجرد خلطه بتراب من "الدر المختار". السلم: ستعرف معنى السلم في بابه إن شاء الله تعالى.
المرعى أي لا يجوز بيع الكلأ وإجارته، أما البيع؛ فلأنه ورد على ما لا يملكه لاشتراك الناس فيه بالحديث، وأما الإجارة؛ فلأنها عقدت على استهلاك عين مباح "البحر الرائق" (۸۳/٦) ، وفي "الدر المختار": هذا إذا نبت
Examples of Invalid Sale
4 - Suspended: such as when one sells the property of another; it confers a ruling contingent upon permission. If the owner permits, it is effective; otherwise it is void.
Examples of Invalid Sale and Its Ruling
Question: Briefly describe the scenario of an invalid sale.
Answer: If one of the two exchanges or both are prohibited or are not owned, then the sale is invalid.
Question: Provide some examples of invalid sale.
Answer: Invalid is the sale of a non-existent thing and what is not property, like spilled blood and carrion, and a free person, and mudamin and malaqih, and the fetus (haml), and the offspring (nitaj), and intentionally omitting the name (of Allah), and invalid is the sale of the skin of a carrion before tanning, and selling a slaughtered sheep mixed with a sheep that died naturally, and selling human hair due to its dignity, and selling pig hair due to its impurity, and invalid is the sale of human feces not overcome by soil, and selling what is not in one's possession except by way of salam, and invalid is the sale of fish not yet caught, and a bird in the air, and invalid is the sale of milk in the udder, pearls in the shell, wool on the back of sheep, and invalid is the sale of wine and pig, and a slave attached to a free person, as well as the sale of grass in the pasture.
Carrion except fish and locusts; al-haml (with sukun on the meem) is the fetus, and nitaj is the offspring of the she-camel's pregnancy, and selling them is invalid. In the Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq: prohibition of mudamin and malaqih and habl al-habla. Mudamin: plural of madmuna, what is in the loins of camels; malaqih: plural of maluqih, what is in their bellies; it is also said the opposite. Habl al-habla: the offspring of the she-camel's offspring. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: it is not permissible to sell the fetus alone without the mother, nor the mother without it. If one sells the fetus and she gives birth before separation, it is not permissible. From al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (6/80): soil; if [feces] is overcome [by soil] it is permissible like dung and pellets; in al-Bahr they are content with mere mixing with soil from al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Salam: you will come to know the meaning of salam in its chapter, God willing.
Al-Mar'a: i.e., it is not permissible to sell grass nor to lease it; as for sale, because it occurs on what is not owned due to the common ownership of people as per the hadith; as for lease, because it is contracted on the consumption of a permissible substance. From al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (6/83). In al-Durr al-Mukhtar: this is if it grows.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with accompanying expansion from later commentaries. It begins by defining invalid sale (bay' batil) in general: when either the price, the object, or both are unlawful or not owned. Then it lists numerous specific examples of invalid sales, including: sale of non-existent items (ma'dum), things not considered property (like blood, carrion), free persons, certain types of unborn animals (mudamin, malaqih, habl al-habla), intentionally omitting the basmalah when slaughtering, untanned carrion skin, mixing permissible and impermissible meat, human hair (respected), pig hair (impure), human feces not mixed with earth, sale of what one does not own unless via salam, uncaptured fish, birds in the air, milk in the udder, pearls in shells, wool on sheep, wine, pigs, combining a slave with a free person, and grass in a common pasture. The commentary then explains specific terms: 'carrion' except fish and locusts; 'haml' is the fetus; 'nitaj' is the offspring; hadith from Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq prohibiting mudamin (what is in the loins of camels) and malaqih (what is in the bellies) and habl al-habla (offspring of the offspring). The commentary notes that selling the fetus alone or the mother alone is not permissible. The discussion of feces indicates that if it is mixed with soil it becomes permissible to sell (like fertilizer). The last part addresses grass: selling or leasing grass in a common pasture is invalid because it is owned collectively by all Muslims; only after one cultivates it by watering and tending does it become his property, and then it may be sold. However, there is a difference of opinion: al-Quduri (and some later scholars) hold that ownership is not achieved by watering alone but only by actual possession (hayazah), but the majority of later scholars (mutakhkhirin) permit the sale.

Key Terms
الكلأ في المرعى (al-kala' fi al-mar'a): Grass growing in a common pasture. Its sale is invalid because it is owned collectively by all Muslims.
الاختلاف (al-ikhtilaf): Disagreement among Hanafi scholars (specifically al-Quduri vs. majority) on the sale of grass after watering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Yes, because watering grants ownership
C) No, because grass cannot be sold even if owned

Key Takeaway
Grass in a common pasture is non-saleable initially, but majority Hanafi opinion permits sale after cultivation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Invalid Sales: Umm Walad, Mudabbar, Mukatab and the Ruling of Batil
Q&A on specific batil sales and their ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which specific sales are batil in Hanafi fiqh and explain the consequence of a batil sale.

Core Idea
A sale is batil if it conflicts with an established right to freedom.

This lesson examines three specific sales that Hanafi jurists unanimously classify as batil (invalid): selling an umm walad, a mudabbar, and a mukatab. We will also explore the general ruling of batil sales and distinguish between absolute and conditional mudabbar. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly applying the law of sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three special slaves
List the three categories: umm walad (mother of the master's child), mudabbar (slave promised freedom after death), and mukatab (slave under a manumission contract). Each has a right to freedom that is established during the master's lifetime.
For the mudabbar, distinguish absolute (e.g., 'you are free after my death') from conditional (e.g., 'if I die on this journey, you are free').
2
Understand why their sale is batil
The passage explains that selling them would nullify their right to freedom. For the umm walad, the hadith 'her child frees her' establishes freedom. For the mudabbar, the cause of freedom is already in effect. For the mukatab, the kitabah contract gives a binding right. Hence, the sale is invalid because it contradicts this right.
The ruling is based on the principle that a right to freedom cannot be overridden by a sale.
3
Handle the ikhtilaf on mukatab's consent
There are two narrations in the Hanafi school: the more apparent (zahir) allows sale if the mukatab consents; the sound (sahih) does not. The majority (mufta bihi) is that even with consent, the sale does not take effect.
This is a intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the relied-upon position is the sahih narration.
4
Apply the general ruling of batil
The passage states: the batil sale does not create ownership. Even if the buyer takes possession, he does not become owner. The contract is void ab initio.
This contrasts with fasid (corrupt) sale, where the contract is potentially valid if the defect is removed.
5
Distinguish other batil sales in the passage
The fish net example: selling fish not yet caught is batil because the object is not owned at the time of contract. The mixed sale example: selling one's own slave together with a mudabbar or another's slave is valid for the owned slave but invalid for the other.
These reinforce the condition that the object must be owned and saleable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة البيع الفساد
السؤال: إذا باع السيد أمّ الولد أو المدبر أو المكاتب ما حكم بيعه؟ الجواب: هذه البيوع كلها باطلة.
السؤال: رجل ضرب شبكة ليصطاد وقال لرجل آخر كل صيد وقع في الشبكة في هذه المرة فهو لك بكذا، ما حكم هذا البيع ؟
الجواب: هذا بيع باطل؛ لأن ما يصطاده بعد عقد المبايعة ليس في ملكه حين العقد. السؤال: جمع بين عبد ومدبر، أو بين عبده وعبد غيره، فباع في بيع واحد، ماذا تقولون فيه ؟ الجواب: يصح البيع في العبد الذي هو ملكه بحصته من الثمن، ولا يصح في المدبر ولا في عبد غيره.

السؤال: بينوا بعض أمثلة البيع الفاسد.
وإن أنبته بسقي وتربية ملكه، وجاز بيعه (عيني)، وقيل: لا، قال ابن عابدين قوله (وقيل: لا)، أي لا يملكه وهو اختيار القدوري؛ لأن الشركة ثابتة وإنما تنقطع بالحيازة، وسوق الماء ليس بحيازة، وعلى الجواز أكثر المشايخ، واختاره الشهيد انتهى. يعني أكثر المشايخ أفتوا بجواز بيع الكلأ الذي أنبته بسقي الأرض وإعدادها للإنبات. باطلة قال في البحر الرائق ((٧٨/٦): أما المدبر وأم الولد فقد صرّح في "الهداية" ببطلان بيعهما، قال: لأن استحقاق العتق قد ثبت لأم الولد لقوله عليها : أعتقها ولدها وسبب الحرية انعقد في حق المدبر في الحال لبطلان الأهلية بعد الموت والمكاتب استحق العتق يدًا على نفسه لازمة في حق المولى، ولو ثبت الملك بالبيع لبطل ذلك كله فلا يجوز، ولو رضي المكاتب بالبيع ففيه روايتان الأظهر الجواز، والمراد بالمدير المطلق دون المقيد؛ فإنه يجوز بيعه ولو باع المكاتب بغير رضاه فأجاز بيعه، لا ينفذ في الصحيح من الرواية، وعليه عامة المشايخ، كذا في " الخانية". والمدبر المطلق هو العبد الذي قال له :مولاه أنت حر بعد موتي، وأما المقيد : فهو العبد الذي قال له مولاه: إن مت في سفري هذا فأنت حر، أو قال: إن مت من مرضي هذا فأنت حر.
Examples of Corrupt Sale
Question: If the master sells the umm walad (mother of a child), or the mudabbar (slave promised freedom after master's death), or the mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), what is the ruling on his sale? Answer: All these sales are invalid.
Question: A man casts a net to fish and says to another: 'Every fish that falls into the net this time is yours for such-and-such.' What is the ruling on this sale? Answer: This is an invalid sale, because what he catches after the contract is not in his possession at the time of the contract.
Question: He combined a slave and a mudabbar, or his own slave and the slave of another, and sold them in one sale. What do you say? Answer: The sale is valid for the slave that he owns for his share of the price, and it is not valid for the mudabbar nor for the slave of another.
Question: And what is the ruling on the invalid sale (batil)? Answer: Its ruling is that the sale does not take effect, and the buyer does not own the sold item even if he takes possession.
Examples of Corrupt Sale and Its Ruling
=
Question: Provide some examples of corrupt sale (fasid).
And if he grows it by watering and tending, he owns it, and its sale is permissible (according to some), and it is said: no. Ibn 'Abidin said: 'And it is said: no' means he does not own it, and this is the choice of al-Quduri, because the partnership (in common pasture) remains and is only severed by actual possession (hayazah), and watering is not possession. The majority of the mashayikh permit it, and al-Shahid chose this. That is, the majority of the mashayikh gave fatwa on the permissibility of selling grass that one has grown by watering the land and preparing it for growth. [The sales of mudabbar and umm walad are] invalid. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (6/78) said: As for the mudabbar and umm walad, it is explicitly stated in al-Hidayah that their sale is invalid. He said: Because the right to freedom is established for the umm walad by the Prophet's statement: 'Her child frees her,' and the cause of freedom is contracted for the mudabbar immediately due to the nullification of capacity after death. The mukatab has established a right to freedom through a binding contract concerning himself with respect to the master. If ownership were established by sale, all that would be nullified, so it is not permissible. If the mukatab consents to the sale, there are two narrations, the more apparent being permissibility. By mudabbar is meant the absolute (mutlaq) one, not the conditional (muqayyad); the conditional one may be sold. If one sells the mukatab without his consent and he subsequently permits it, it does not take effect according to the sound narration, and this is the view of the majority of the mashayikh, as in al-Khaniyyah. The absolute mudabbar is the slave to whom his master says: 'You are free after my death.' The conditional mudabbar is the slave to whom his master says: 'If I die on this journey, you are free,' or: 'If I die from this illness, you are free.'
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage addresses specific cases of invalid sales (batil) and briefly introduces the concept of corrupt sale (fasid). The first Q&A establishes that selling an umm walad (a slave woman who has borne her master's child), a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom after the master's death), or a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) is batil. The reason, as elaborated from al-Hidayah via al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, is that these slaves have a right to freedom that would be extinguished by a sale. For the umm walad, the hadith 'her child frees her' establishes her eventual freedom; the mudabbar's freedom is fixed upon the master's death; the mukatab has a contract that gives him a right to freedom. Selling them would contradict these rights. However, if the mukatab consents to the sale, there is a difference of opinion: the more apparent view (zahir al-riwaya) allows it, but the sound narration (sahih) is that even with consent it does not take effect, and this is the view of the majority of scholars. Also, only an absolutely declared mudabbar is included; a conditional mudabbar (e.g., 'if I die on this journey, you are free') may be sold because the condition may not occur. The second Q&A involves selling fish that will be caught: the sale is batil because the object is not owned at the time of contract. The third Q&A addresses a mixed sale: if someone sells his own slave together with a mudabbar or another's slave, the sale is valid for his own slave (for his share of the price) but invalid for the other, because the mudabbar or another's slave cannot be sold. The fourth Q&A gives the ruling of a batil sale: it does not create ownership, and even if the buyer takes possession, he does not become owner. The passage then begins to discuss examples of fasid (corrupt) sale, but the text immediately goes back to a continuation of the discussion on grass and then a reiteration of the ruling on mudabbar and umm walad from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The commentary by Ibn 'Abidin notes that although al-Quduri held that grass is not owned by watering alone, the majority of later scholars (mutakhkhirin) permit its sale. This illustrates the distinction between batil and fasid in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has given birth to a child from her master, thereby acquiring the right to freedom upon her master's death.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whom the master declares free after his own death, either absolutely or conditionally. Absolute mudabbar cannot be sold; conditional may be sold.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who enters a contract with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments. The slave retains ownership of his earnings.
الباطل (al-batil): An invalid sale that does not produce any legal effects; it is void ab initio.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is valid because the condition may not occur
B) It is invalid like an absolute mudabbar
C) It is valid only with the slave's consent
D) It is batil because any tadbir invalidates sale

Key Takeaway
Sales that conflict with an established right to freedom are batil, and a batil sale does not transfer ownership even if possession is taken.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Invalid Sales: Umm Walad, Mudabbar, and Mukatab
Lists and explains three types of batil sales with their reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of slaves whose sale is batil in Hanafi fiqh and explain why, citing the underlying rationale of pre-existing emancipation rights.

Core Idea
A sale is batil if the subject's ownership is encumbered with an irrevocable right to freedom.

This lesson examines three categories of slaves—umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab—whose sale is ruled batil by the Hanafi school. Understanding why these sales are invalid is key to grasping the principle that ownership must be complete and transferable for a sale to be valid. We will also explore the distinctions between absolute and restricted mudabbar and the effect of the mukatab's consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The passage names umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab. What do they share? Each has a recognized right to emancipation that conflicts with the transfer of ownership through sale.
These are not the only batil sales, but they are clear examples of invalidating pre-existing rights.
2
Understand the reasoning for each
For umm walad, the hadith 'Her child frees her' establishes her right. For mudabbar, the cause of freedom is triggered immediately with the promise. For mukatab, the kitabah contract gives him a binding claim to freedom. A sale would nullify these rights.
3
Distinguish absolute vs restricted mudabbar
The passage clarifies that only the absolute mudabbar (unconditional promise) cannot be sold; the restricted mudabbar (conditional on an event) can be sold because the promise is not yet fully binding.
This distinction is important for practical application.
4
Analyze the mukatab's consent
There are two narrations on selling a mukatab with his consent. The preferred view (the 'most apparent') permits it, but without consent the sale is ineffective. This shows that the mukatab's right is not absolute but can be waived.
The majority of scholars follow this preferred view.
5
Apply the ruling to combined sales
If a sale includes both a valid item (e.g., the master's own slave) and an invalid one (e.g., a mudabbar or another's slave), the sale is valid only for the valid item proportionally. The invalid part makes the whole contract batil only for that part.
This is based on the principle that a batil element does not necessarily void the entire contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: إذا باع السيد أمّ الولد أو المدبر أو المكاتب ما حكم بيعه؟ الجواب: هذه البيوع كلها باطلة.
السؤال: رجل ضرب شبكة ليصطاد وقال لرجل آخر كل صيد وقع في الشبكة في هذه المرة فهو لك بكذا، ما حكم هذا البيع ؟
الجواب: هذا بيع باطل؛ لأن ما يصطاده بعد عقد المبايعة ليس في ملكه حين العقد.
السؤال: جمع بين عبد ومدبر، أو بين عبده وعبد غيره، فباع في بيع واحد، ماذا تقولون فيه ؟
الجواب: يصح البيع في العبد الذي هو ملكه بحصته من الثمن، ولا يصح في المدبر ولا في عبد غيره.
باطلة قال في البحر الرائق ((78/6): أما المدبر وأم الولد فقد صرّح في "الهداية" ببطلان بيعهما، قال: لأن استحقاق العتق قد ثبت لأم الولد لقوله عليه السلام : أعتقها ولدها وسبب الحرية انعقد في حق المدبر في الحال لبطلان الأهلية بعد الموت والمكاتب استحق العتق يدًا على نفسه لازمة في حق المولى، ولو ثبت الملك بالبيع لبطل ذلك كله فلا يجوز، ولو رضي المكاتب بالبيع ففيه روايتان الأظهر الجواز، والمراد بالمدبر المطلق دون المقيد؛ فإنه يجوز بيعه ولو باع المكاتب بغير رضاه فأجاز بيعه، لا ينفذ في الصحيح من الرواية، وعليه عامة المشايخ، كذا في " الخانية". والمدبر المطلق هو العبد الذي قال له مولاه: أنت حر بعد موتي، وأما المقيد : فهو العبد الذي قال له مولاه: إن مت في سفري هذا فأنت حر، أو قال: إن مت من مرضي هذا فأنت حر.
Question: If the master sells umm walad (mother of a child) or mudabbar (a slave promised emancipation after death) or mukatab (a slave under contract of emancipation), what is the ruling of their sale? Answer: All these sales are invalid (batil). Question: A man casts a net to fish and says to another man, 'Every fish caught in this net at this time is yours for such-and-such price.' What is the ruling of this sale? Answer: This sale is invalid because what he catches after the contract was not in his ownership at the time of the contract. Question: He combines a slave and a mudabbar, or his slave and another's slave, and sells them in one sale. What do you say about it? Answer: The sale is valid for the slave that is his ownership for his share of the price, and it is not valid for the mudabbar nor for the other's slave. Invalid, said in al-Bahr al-Raiq (6:78): As for mudabbar and umm walad, it is explicitly stated in al-Hidayah that their sale is invalid. He said: Because the entitlement to emancipation is established for umm walad by his saying, peace be upon him, 'Her child frees her,' and the cause of freedom is established for the mudabbar in the present due to the invalidity of capacity after death, and the mukatab deserves emancipation by a binding commitment on himself regarding the master. If ownership were established by sale, all that would become invalid, so it is not permissible. If the mukatab consents to the sale, there are two narrations: the most apparent is permissibility. The intended mudabbar is the absolute one, not the restricted; for it is permissible to sell him. If the mukatab sells without his consent and then he permits it, it is not effective according to the correct narration, and upon this are the majority of scholars, as in al-Khaniyyah. The absolute mudabbar is the slave to whom his master says, 'You are free after my death.' As for the restricted, it is the slave to whom his master says, 'If I die on this journey of mine, you are free,' or said, 'If I die from this illness of mine, you are free.'
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents three examples of invalid (batil) sales. First, the sale of an umm walad, mudabbar, or mukatab is batil because each of these has an existing right to emancipation that would be nullified by transferring ownership. The author quotes al-Bahr al-Raiq explaining that the sale of umm walad is invalid due to the prophetic hadith 'Her child frees her'; for mudabbar, emancipation is established immediately in cause; for mukatab, the contract of kitabah gives him a binding right. Regarding combined sales, when a man sells his own slave along with a mudabbar or another's slave, the sale is valid for his own slave (for a proportional share of the price) but invalid for the other item. The passage also clarifies the distinction between absolute mudabbar (promised upon death unconditionally) and restricted mudabbar (conditioned on an event), noting that the restricted mudabbar can be sold. For mukatab, consent matters: if he consents, the sale is valid according to the preferred narration; otherwise, it is not effective. These rulings illustrate that batil sales are those fundamentally defective in their very basis, such as lack of ownership or capacity.

Key Terms
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master; she becomes free upon the master's death.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave promised emancipation after the master's death; the promise is binding once made.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave under a contract of kitabah (emancipation in exchange for payments); he has a binding right to freedom.
بيع باطل (bay' batil): A sale that is inherently invalid due to a fundamental defect, such as lack of ownership; it does not transfer ownership and has no legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of an umm walad considered batil in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because she is not owned by the master.
B) Because she has a pre-existing right to emancipation that would be nullified by sale.
C) Because the master cannot sell anything he owns.
D) Because the sale requires witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The sale of umm walad, absolute mudabbar, and mukatab (without consent) is batil because each possesses a pre-existing right to emancipation that a valid sale would violate.

Ruling of a Batil Sale: No Contract, No Ownership
Ruling statement on batil sale effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two legal consequences of a batil sale and distinguish batil from fasid in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A batil sale never becomes a valid contract; possession does not transfer ownership.

In Hanafi fiqh, a sale that is fundamentally defective is called 'batil'. This lesson examines its legal effects. Unlike a fasid (corrupt) sale, which may be rectified, a batil sale is void from the start. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly classifying transactions and determining ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question posed
The text asks about the ruling (hukm) of a batil sale. Recognize that the answer covers two distinct legal consequences.
The answer is structured as two separate statements: one about contract formation, one about ownership.
2
Extract the two consequences
First consequence: the sale does not 'contract' (in'aqad) – it is not validly formed. Second consequence: the buyer does not own the item even if he takes possession.
In Hanafi law, taking possession (qabd) alone does not cure the fundamental defect.
3
Understand the term 'batil'
Batil means void ab initio. Contrast with 'fasid' (corrupt) where the sale may become valid by removing the defect. Here, no correction is possible.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh; other schools may treat batil/fasid similarly.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a sale of stolen goods. The contract lacks seller's ownership – an essential element. Hence it is batil. The buyer does not own the item even if he took delivery.
The ruling protects the original owner's right and prevents unjust enrichment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: وما حكم البيع الباطل؟
الجواب: حكمه عدم انعقاد البيع. وعدم ملك المشتري المبيع إذا قبضه.
Question: And what is the ruling of the invalid (batil) sale? Answer: Its ruling is that the sale does not contract, and the buyer does not own the sold item if he takes possession of it.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This is a concise ruling on the legal effect of a batil (invalid) sale. The answer states two consequences: (1) The contract does not come into existence (it never 'contracted'), meaning it is void ab initio. (2) Even if the buyer physically takes possession of the item, he does not become its owner. This is because the contract lacked a fundamental element (such as seller's ownership or capacity), so it is as if no agreement occurred. In Hanafi fiqh, a batil sale is distinguished from a fasid (corrupt) sale: the latter may have some effect if certain defects are rectified, whereas a batil sale has no legal effect whatsoever. This ruling forms a memorization anchor for students to understand the core difference between batil and fasid.

Key Terms
بيع باطل (bay' batil): A sale that is fundamentally defective; it never becomes a valid contract.
انعقاد البيع (in'aqad al-bay'): The formation or coming into existence of the sale contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He becomes the owner immediately.
B) He becomes the owner if the seller confirms the sale.
C) He does not become the owner of the item.
D) He must return the item but can keep it if he pays the price again.

Key Takeaway
A batil sale has no legal effect: it does not create a contract, and the buyer does not own the item even if in possession.

Six Types of Corrupt (Fasid) Sales
Lists six corrupt sales with definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define the six types of fasid (corrupt) sales in Hanafi fiqh, and state the general cause of corruption.

Core Idea
Sales based on chance or ignorance are corrupt (fasid) — the Prophet ﷺ forbade them.

In this passage, the author lists six classic examples of corrupt sales that were known in pre-Islamic Arabia and explicitly prohibited by the Prophet ﷺ. Although each has its own name and form, they all share a common defect: ignorance (jahl) and reliance on chance (khatar). Understanding these examples clarifies the Hanafi principle that valid sales require certainty and mutual consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six corrupt sales
From the passage, list the six named types: muzabana, mulamasa, munabadha, bay' bi-ilqa al-hajar, sale with exception of unborn young, and partial sales (yard of cloth, beam, one garment from two).
These are the specific categories given.
2
Define each sale
Using the author's explanations, define each type: muzabana (fresh fruit sold for dried by estimation), mulamasa (sale by touch without inspection), munabadha (sale by throwing garments), bay' bi-ilqa al-hajar (sale by throwing a stone), istithna' al-haml (excepting unborn young), and partial sales when division harms.
Each definition highlights the defect.
3
Identify the common factor
The passage states the prohibition is due to ignorance (jahl) and linking ownership to chance (khatar). All six types involve one or both of these elements.
This is the 'illa (effective cause) for the ruling.
4
Note the exception for partial sales
The text adds that if the seller cuts the yard or removes the beam before the buyer dissolves the contract, the sale becomes valid. This shows that fasid can become sahih if the defect is removed.
Different from batil, which cannot be rectified.
5
Apply the ruling to modern scenarios
Consider if any contemporary sale resembles these types, e.g., a lucky draw sale or a sale where the item is randomly selected. Such sales are fasid unless the defect is eliminated before dissolution.
The principle remains relevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: بينوا بعض أمثلة البيع الفاسد.
الجواب: يفسد بيع المزابنة والملامسة والمنابذة والبيع بإلقاء الحجر، كما يفسد إذا باع جارية أو دابة واستثنى حملها، أو باع ذراعا من ثوب، أو جذعا من سقف، أو ثوبا من ثوبين.
بالقاء الحجر: المزابنة : بيع الرطبة على النخيل بتمر مقطوع مثل كيله تقديرا، ومثله بيع العنب على شجرة الزبيب. والملامسة: هو أن يلمس كل واحد من المتعاقدين ثوب صاحبه من غير تأمل؛ ليلزم اللامس البيع من غير خيار له عند الرؤية، وهذا بأن يكون مثلا في ظلمة، أو يكون الثوب مطويا مرئيا يتفقان على أنه إذا لمسه فقد باعه منه وفساده لتعليق التمليك على أنه متى لمسه وجب البيع وسقط خيار المجلس والمنابذة: أن ينبذ كل واحد منهما ثوبه إلى آخر ولا ينظر كل واحد منهما إلى ثوب صاحبه على جعل النبذ بيعًا، وهذه كانت بيوعا يتعارفونها في الجاهلية. وإلقاء الحجر أن يلقي حصاة وثمة أثواب، فأي ثوب وقع عليه كان البيع بلا تأمل ورؤية، ولا خيار بعد ذلك، ولا بد أن يسبق تراوضهما يعني تساومهما على الثمن ولا فرق بين كون المبيع معينا أو غير معين، ومعنى النهي ما في كل من الجهالة وتعليق التمليك بالخطر؛ فإنه في معنى إذا وقع حجري على ثوب فقد بعته منك، بعتنيه بكذا، أو إذا نبذته أو لمسته كذا في "الفتح" من "الدر المختار" و"رد المحتار". ولقد نهى النبي ﷺ عن هذه البيوع، راجع "مشكاة المصابيح" (باب المنهي عنها من البيوع). واستثنى حملها راجع "منحة الخالق على البحر الرائق" (6/95). من ثوب في "تنوير الأبصار": وذراع من ثوب يضره التبعيض، قال في "الدر المختار: فلو سلم وقطع قبل فسخ المشتري عاد صحيحا، ولم يضره القطع ككرباس جاز لاندفاع المانع. وفي "الهداية: ولو قطع البائع الذراع أو قلع الجذع قبل أن يفسخ المشتري يعود صحيحا لزوال المفسد
Question: Explain some examples of corrupt (fasid) sales. Answer: The following sales become corrupt: muzabana (selling fresh fruit on trees for dry fruit by estimation), mulamasa (touching garment), munabadha (throwing garment), sale by throwing a stone, as well as selling a slave woman or an animal while excepting its unborn young, selling a yard of cloth, a beam from a ceiling, or a garment from two garments. Regarding throwing a stone: Muzabana is selling fresh dates on the palm trees for cut dates by estimation of their measure; similarly selling grapes on the vine for raisins. Mulamasa is that each of the two contracting parties touches the other's garment without examination, to bind the toucher to the sale without any option to see; this is, for example, in darkness, or when the garment is folded but visible, and they agree that if he touches it he has sold it to him. Its corruption is due to linking the transfer of ownership to a condition: whenever he touches it, the sale becomes binding and the option of the gathering falls. Munabadha is that each one throws his garment to the other, and neither looks at the other's garment, making the throw a sale. These were sales known among them in the Jahiliyyah. Throwing a stone is that he throws a pebble, and there are garments; whichever garment the pebble hits becomes the sale without examination or seeing, and no option thereafter. It is necessary that their negotiation precedes, meaning haggling over the price; there is no difference whether the sold item is specific or unspecified. The meaning of the prohibition is the ignorance and the linking of ownership to chance. It is like saying, 'If my stone falls on a garment, I have sold it to you for such-and-such,' or 'If I threw it or touched it,' as in al-Fath from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar. The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade these sales; see Mishkat al-Masabih (Chapter on forbidden sales). As for excepting the young, see Minhat al-Khaliq 'ala al-Bahr al-Raiq (6:95). Regarding 'a yard from a cloth' in Tanwir al-Absar: 'A yard of cloth that is harmed by division.' Al-Durr al-Mukhtar says: 'If the seller delivers and cuts before the buyer dissolves, it becomes valid, and the cutting does not harm it.' In al-Hidayah: 'If the seller cuts the yard or removes the beam before the buyer dissolves, it becomes valid because the corrupting factor has been removed.'
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage enumerates examples of corrupt (fasid) sales. The author first lists six types: muzabana, mulamasa, munabadha, sale by throwing a stone, sale with exception of unborn young, and partial sales (yard of cloth, beam from ceiling, one garment from two). Then he explains each in detail, referencing authoritative Hanafi sources. The common factors causing corruption are ignorance (jahl) and linking ownership to chance (khatar), which are akin to gambling. Muzabana is selling fresh dates on trees for dried dates by estimation, which involves uncertainty of measure. Mulamasa and munabadha are pre-Islamic practices where the contract was concluded by touch or throw without proper inspection. Throwing a stone similarly relies on random selection. The exception of an unborn animal (habal al-habalah) is corrupt because it is uncertain. Partial sales (e.g., a yard from a large cloth) are corrupt when the division harms the item, but if the seller cuts and delivers before the buyer cancels, the contract becomes valid because the defect (uncertainty) is removed. The passage emphasizes that the Prophet ﷺ explicitly forbade these sales, and the Hanafi school has preserved these rulings with detailed conditions.

Key Terms
المزابنة (al-muzabana): Selling fresh fruit on trees for dry fruit by estimation, leading to uncertainty of quantity.
الملامسة (al-mulamasa): A sale concluded by touching the garment without inspection; the touch binds the sale.
المنابذة (al-munabadha): A sale concluded by throwing garments to each other without examination.
بيع بإلقاء الحجر (bay' bi-ilqa al-hajar): A sale concluded by throwing a stone; whichever item it hits is sold.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is defective due to a missing condition or element that can be rectified; it is invalid unless the defect is removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It involves a stone that is not owned.
B) It links ownership to chance and involves ignorance.
C) It requires prior negotiation.
D) It is only corrupt if the item is unspecified.

Key Takeaway
The six corrupt sales are prohibited due to ignorance and chance; they can become valid only if the defect is removed before the buyer cancels.

Five Conditions for a Corrupting Stipulation
Defines the five conditions that make a stipulation corrupting in a sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the five Hanafi conditions to determine whether a stipulation in a sale makes it fasid.

Core Idea
A stipulation corrupts a sale only if it meets all five: not required, incompatible, beneficial, deserver, uncustomary and unpermitted.

In Hanafi sales law, not every extra stipulation invalidates the contract. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq sets out the precise cumulative criteria that turn a stipulation into a corrupting factor. Understanding these five conditions is essential for distinguishing valid from invalid sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
The passage opens with whether stipulations corrupt a sale. Recognize that the answer defines the exact conditions for corruption.
The question is answered positively but with qualifications.
2
List the five conditions
Extract the five conditions from the answer: not required by contract, not compatible, beneficial to a party or subject, beneficiary is deserving, and not customary or legally allowed.
All five must be present for the stipulation to corrupt.
3
Understand the negation
If any condition is missing, the stipulation does not corrupt. For example, if the stipulation is required by the contract (like detaining for payment), the sale remains valid.
The passage gives this counter-example explicitly.
4
Apply to a scenario
Test a stipulation against each condition. Only if all five are satisfied does the sale become fasid.
Practice with examples like forbidding resale (incompatible) vs. installment payment (customary).
5
Note the authority
The passage cites 'sahib al-Bahr' (Ibn Nujaym), confirming that this is the mufta bihi position in the later Hanafi school.
The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: هل يفسد البيع بشروط ؟
الجواب: نعم، يفسد بشرط لا يقتضيه العقد، ولا يلائمه، وفيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين، أو للمعقود عليه، وهو من أهل الاستحقاق، ولم يجر العرف به ولم يرد الشرع بجوازه.
قال صاحب البحر (6/92) بعد ذكر هذه الشروط فلا بد في كون الشرط مفسدا للبيع من هذه الشرائط الخمسة، فإن كان الشرط يقتضيه العقد، فإنه لا يفسد كشرط أن يحبس المبيع إلى قبض الثمن ونحوه.
Question: Does a sale become corrupt through stipulations? Answer: Yes, it becomes corrupt by a condition that is not required by the contract, nor suitable to it, and contains a benefit for one of the two contractors or for the subject matter, and the person benefiting is deserving, and custom has not been established with it, nor has law permitted it. The author of al-Bahr said (6:92): After mentioning these conditions, it is necessary for the condition to be corrupting that these five conditions exist. If the condition is required by the contract, then it does not corrupt, such as the condition to detain the sold item until receipt of the price and similar.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage addresses when a stipulation (shart) in a sale contract makes it corrupt (fasid). The answer sets five cumulative conditions for a stipulation to render the sale fasid: (1) It is not demanded by the contract itself (i.e., not a natural consequence like delivery). (2) It is not compatible with the contract's nature (e.g., a condition that contradicts the effect of sale). (3) It confers a benefit to one of the parties or the subject matter. (4) The person benefiting is entitled to that benefit (ahl al-istihqaq). (5) Neither custom nor Islamic law permits it. If a stipulation meets all five, the sale becomes fasid. Conversely, if the stipulation is required by the contract (like detaining the item until payment), it does not corrupt. The author quotes from al-Bahr al-Raiq emphasizing that these five conditions are necessary for a stipulation to be corrupting. This is a key legal principle in Hanafi sales law, distinguishing between permissible and impermissible stipulations.

Key Terms
شرط مفسد (shart mufsid): A stipulation that makes a sale corrupt (fasid) if it meets the five conditions mentioned.
لا يقتضيه العقد (la yaqtadihi al-'aqd): The stipulation is not naturally required or implied by the contract's nature.
لا يلائمه (la yula'imuhu): The stipulation is not compatible with the contract's purpose or effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the five conditions for a stipulation to corrupt a sale?
A) The stipulation is not required by the contract.
B) The stipulation is beneficial to one party.
C) The stipulation is customary.
D) The stipulation is not legally permitted.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, a stipulation only corrupts the sale if it meets all five conditions: not required by contract, not compatible, beneficial, the beneficiary is deserving, and neither customary nor legally permitted.
