The Prohibited Sales: Muzabanah, Mulamasah, Munabadhah, Ilqa' al-Hajar
Listing prohibited Jahiliyyah sales with definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define each of the four prohibited Jahiliyyah sales and explain why they are considered fasid due to gharar and khatar.

Core Idea
These four sales are invalid because they combine ignorance and gambling.

The Hanafi school classifies certain sales as fasid (invalid) due to inherent uncertainty (gharar) and chance (khatar). The text introduces four sales from the pre-Islamic period that the Prophet ﷺ prohibited. Understanding why these are invalid gives us a foundational tool for evaluating modern transactions with similar defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four sales
List the four prohibited sales by name: muzabanah, mulamasah, munabadhah, and ilqa' al-hajar. Know the Arabic terms.
These are found at the beginning of the passage.
2
Understand the mechanism of each
For each type, describe what happens in the transaction. For example, muzabanah is selling fresh fruit on trees for dry fruit by estimation. Mulamasah is touching a garment to finalize the sale without inspection. Munabadhah is throwing garments at each other to conclude a sale. Ilqa' al-hajar involves throwing a pebble onto garments to determine the sold item.
The passage provides definitions for each.
3
Recognize the common defect
Identify that all these sales share two defects: jahalah (ignorance/uncertainty about the item or price) and khatar (suspending ownership on a chance event). The text explicitly states: 'the meaning of the prohibition is what is in each of ignorance and suspending ownership to chance.'
These are the core reasons for invalidity.
4
Note the evidence
The passage mentions that the Prophet ﷺ forbade these sales. Although no specific hadith is quoted, it directs to Mishkat al-Masabih. This hadith is considered mutawatir in meaning in the Hanafi school.
The prohibition is established by sunnah.
5
State the Hanafi ruling
All four are fasid (invalid) in the Hanafi school. They are not batil (void ab initio) because if the defect is removed before rejection (e.g., the buyer inspects and accepts), the sale could become valid. However, the default ruling is that they are invalid.
Fasid is a distinct category in Hanafi fiqh.

أمثلة البيع الفاسد وحكمه
الجواب: يفسد بيع المزابنة والملامسة والمنابذة والبيع بإلقاء الحجر، كما يفسد إذا باع جارية أو دابة واستثنى حملها، أو باع ذراعا من ثوب، أو جذعا من سقف، أو ثوبا من ثوبين.
السؤال: هل يفسد البيع بشروط ؟
الجواب: نعم، يفسد بشرط لا يقتضيه العقد، ولا يلائمه، وفيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين، أو للمعقود عليه، وهو من أهل الاستحقاق، ولم يجر العرف به ولم يرد الشرع بجوازه.
بالقاء الحجر: المزابنة : بيع الرطبة على النخيل بتمر مقطوع مثل كيله تقديرا، ومثله بيع العنب على شجرة الزبيب. والملامسة: هو أن يلمس كل وحد من المتعاقدين ثوب صاحبه من غير تأمل؛ ليلزم اللامس البيع من غير خيار له عند الرؤية، وهذا بأن يكون مثلا في ظلمة، أو يكون الثوب مطويا مرئيا يتفقان على أنه إذا لمسه فقد باعه منه وفساده لتعليق التمليك على أنه متى لمسه وجب البيع وسقط خيار المجلس والمنابذة: أن ينبذ كل واحد منهما ثوبه إلى آخر ولا ينظر كل واحد منهما إلى ثوب صاحبه على جعل النبذ بيعًا، وهذه كانت بيوعا يتعارفونها في الجاهلية.
وإلقاء الحجر أن يلقي حصاة وثمة أثواب، فأي ثوب وقع عليه كان البيع بلا تأمل ورؤية، ولا خيار بعد ذلك، ولا بد أن يسبق تراوضهما يعني تساومهما على الثمن ولا فرق بين كون المبيع معينا أو غير معين، ومعنى النهي ما في كل من الجهالة وتعليق التمليك بالخطر؛ فإنه في معنى إذا وقع حجري على ثوب فقد بعته منك، بعتنيه بكذا، أو إذا نبذته أو لمسته كذا في "الفتح" من "الدر المختار" و"رد المحتار". ولقد نهى النبي عن هذه البيوع، راجع "مشكاة المصابيح" (باب المنهي عنها من البيوع).
واستثنى حملها راجع "منحة الخالق على البحر الرائق" (٩٥/٦). من ثوب في "تنوير الأبصار": وذراع من ثوب يضره التبعيض، قال في "الدر المختار: فلو سلم وقطع قبل فسخ المشتري عاد صحيحا، ولم يضره القطع ككرباس جاز لاندفاع المانع. وفي "الهداية: ولو قطع البائع الذراع أو قلع الجذع قبل أن يفسخ المشتري يعود صحيحا لزوال المفسد من ثوبين البيع فاسد فيه الجهالة المبيع، وكذا يفسد البيع إذا باع من عبدين، كما ذكره في "البحر" (٨٣/٦). بجوازه: قال صاحب البحر" (٩٢/٦) بعد ذكر هذه الشروط فلا بد في كون الشرط مفسدا للبيع من هذه الشرائط الخمسة، فإن كان الشرط يقتضيه العقد، فإنه لا يفسد كشرط أن يحبس المبيع إلى قبض الثمن ونحوه،

كتاب البيوع
أمثلة البيع الفاسد وحكمه
السؤال: بينوا بعض الشروط التي تفسد البيع. الجواب: إذا باع عبدا على أن يعتقه المشتري أو يدبره، أو يكاتبه، أو على أن يستخدمه البائع شهرا، أو باع أمة على أن يستولدها المشتري، أو دارا على أن يسكنها البائع مدة معلومة، أو باع عينا على أن لا يسلم البائع إلى رأس الشهر، أو على أن يقرضه المشتري درهما، أو على أن يُهدي له شيئا ، فإن هذه الشروط تفسد البيع. السؤال: وما تقولون في رجل اشترى ثوبا على أن يقطعه البائع ويخيطه قميصا أو قباء، أو اشترى نعلا على أن يحذوها البائع أو يضع عليها شراكا؟
الجواب: هذا البيع فاسد في هذه الصور. السؤال: رجلان تبايعا وجعل المشتري الثمن مؤجلا إلى النيروز أو إلى المهرجان وقبله البائع، أو جعلا الأجل إلى صوم النصارى، أو فطر اليهود، ما حكم هذا البيع ؟
الجواب: هذا البيع فاسد إذا لم يعرف المتبايعان هذه الآجال.
فإن كان لا يقتضيه لكن ثبت تصحيحه شرعا فلا مرد له كشرط الأجل في الثمن وفي المبيع المسلم وشرط الخيار لا يفسده، وإن كان متعارفا كشراء النعل على أن يحذوه البائع أو يشركها فهو جائز، وإن كان ملائما للبيع لا يفسده، كالبيع بشرط كفيل بالثمن إذا كان حاضرا وقبلها، أو غائباً فحضر وقبل قبل التفرق. وقوله: (أو فيه نفع للمبيع، وهو من أهل (الاستحقاق بأن يكون آدميا فلو لم يكن كشرط أن لا يركب الدابة المبيعة لم يكن مفسدا. من "الدر المختار".
شراكا هذا ما اختاره القدوري، وقال صاحب "الكنز": صح بيع نعل على أن يحذوه ويشركه. قال صاحب "البحر": والقياس فساده لما فيه من النفع للمشتري مع كون العقد لا يقتضيه، وما ذكره جواب الاستحسان للتعامل، وفي الخروج عن العادة حرج بين بخلاف اشتراط خياطة الثوب لعدم العادة فبقي على أصل القياس، وتسمير القبقاب كتشريك النعل كما في "فتح القدير"، وفي "البزازية" : اشترى ثوبا أو خفا خلقا على أن يرقعه البائع ويخرزه ويسلمه صح للعرف، ومعنى يحذوه يقطعه (٩٥/٦). هذه الآجال: قال صاحب "البحر": أي لا يجوز البيع، وهو فاسد لجهالة الأجل، وهي مفضية إلى المنازعة في البيع لابتنائها على المماكسة، إلا إذا كانا يعرفانه لكونه معلوما عندهما، أو كان التأجيل إلى فطر النصارى =
Book of Sales. Examples of invalid sale and its ruling. Answer: The sale of muzabanah, mulamasah, munabadhah, and the sale by throwing a stone are invalidated, as it is invalidated if one sells a slave girl or an animal and excludes its fetus, or sells a cubit of a garment, or a beam of a roof, or one garment from two garments. Question: Is the sale invalidated by conditions? Answer: Yes, it is invalidated by a condition that is not required by the contract, nor is it suitable for it, and there is benefit in it for one of the two contracting parties, or for the object of the contract, and the beneficiary is worthy of the benefit, and custom has not been established with it, nor has the Sharia permitted it. By throwing a stone: Muzabanah: selling fresh dates on the palm trees for cut dry dates estimated to be the same measure, and likewise selling grapes on the vine for raisins. Mulamasah: that each of the two contracting parties touches the other's garment without reflection; so that the touching obligates the sale with no option for him upon seeing it. This is, for example, in darkness, or if the garment is folded and visible they agree that if he touches it, he has sold it to him. Its invalidity is due to suspending ownership on the condition that whenever he touches it, the sale becomes obligatory and the option of the session falls. Munabadhah: that each of them throws his garment to the other and neither looks at the other's garment, making the throwing a sale. These were sales practiced in the Jahiliyyah. Throwing a stone: that one throws a pebble and there are garments, then whichever garment it falls on, the sale is without reflection and sight, and no option after that. There must be prior negotiation meaning they haggled over the price, and there is no difference whether the sold item is specific or unspecified. The meaning of the prohibition is what is in each of ignorance (jahalah) and suspending ownership to chance (khatar); for it is in the meaning of 'if my stone falls on a garment, I have sold it to you, you have bought it from me for such and such' or 'if I throw it or touch it such', as in al-Fath from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade these sales, see Mishkat al-Masabih (Chapter on Prohibited Sales). Excluding its fetus: see Minhat al-Khaliq ala al-Bahr al-Raiq (6/95). About a cubit of a garment in Tanwir al-Absar: A cubit of a garment harms it by division. He said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: If he delivers and cuts before the buyer cancels, it becomes valid, and cutting does not harm it like cotton cloth (karbas), it is permissible because the impediment is removed. And in al-Hidayah: If the seller cuts the cubit or uproots the beam before the buyer cancels, it becomes valid because the cause of invalidity is removed. From two garments: The sale is invalid due to ignorance of the sold item. Likewise, the sale is invalid if one sells from two slaves, as mentioned in al-Bahr (6/83). About its permissibility: The author of al-Bahr (6/92) said after mentioning these conditions: It is necessary for a condition to be invalidating of the sale to have these five conditions. If the condition is required by the contract, it does not invalidate, like the condition that the seller keep the sold item until receipt of the price, etc. Book of Sales. Examples of invalid sale and its ruling. Question: Explain some conditions that invalidate the sale. Answer: If he sells a slave on condition that the buyer free him, or make him a mudabbar, or make a kitabah, or on condition that the seller use him for a month, or sells a slave girl on condition that the buyer have children from her, or a house on condition that the seller live in it for a known period, or sells an item on condition that the seller not deliver until the beginning of the month, or on condition that the buyer lend him a dirham, or on condition that the buyer give him a gift, then these conditions invalidate the sale. Question: What do you say about a man who buys a garment on condition that the seller cut and sew it as a shirt or a cloak, or buys a sandal on condition that the seller cut it (fashion it) or put a strap on it? Answer: This sale is invalid in these cases. Question: Two men made a sale and the buyer made the price deferred to Nowruz or to Mihrajan and the seller accepted it, or they made the term to the fasting of Christians or the breaking of fast of Jews, what is the ruling of this sale? Answer: This sale is invalid if the two parties do not know these terms. If it is not required by the contract but its validity is established by Sharia then there is no rejection for it, like the condition of a term in the price and in the salam sale, and the condition of option does not invalidate it. If it is customary, like buying a sandal on condition that the seller cut it or put a strap on it, it is permissible. If it is suitable for the sale, it does not invalidate it, like a sale with the condition of a guarantor for the price if he is present and accepts, or if he is absent then comes and accepts before parting. And his statement: 'or there is benefit for the sold item, and it is worthy of (the benefit) by being a human being', so if not, like a condition that the buyer not ride the sold animal, it is not invalidating. From al-Durr al-Mukhtar. 'Straps': this is what al-Quduri chose, and the author of al-Kanz said: The sale of a sandal on condition that he cut it and put a strap on it is valid. The author of al-Bahr said: Qiyas is its invalidity due to the benefit for the buyer while the contract does not require it, and what he mentioned is an answer of istihsan due to custom ('urf), and going against custom involves clear hardship, unlike stipulating the tailoring of a garment due to lack of custom, so it remains on the original qiyas. And nailing a clog (taqbiq) is like putting a strap on a sandal as in Fath al-Qadir, and in al-Bazzaziyyah: He bought a worn-out garment or khuff on condition that the seller patch it, stitch it, and deliver it, it is valid due to custom. And the meaning of yahdhuhu (fashion it) is yqta'uhu (cut it) (6/95). 'These terms': The author of al-Bahr said: That is, the sale is not permissible, it is invalid due to ignorance of the term (ajal), which leads to dispute in the sale because it is based on haggling, unless they both know it because it is known to them, or if the term is to the breaking of fast of Christians =
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is from the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Buyu') and discusses invalid sales (bay' fasid) and conditions that invalidate a sale. It begins by listing four prohibited sales from the Jahiliyyah: muzabanah (selling fresh fruit on trees for dry fruit by estimation), mulamasah (sale by touching without inspection), munabadhah (sale by throwing garments), and sale by throwing a stone (where the garment the stone lands on is sold). All these are invalid due to ignorance (jahalah) and gambling (khatar). The text then explains that an invalid sale also occurs when selling an animal while excluding its fetus, or selling a part of a garment or a beam from a roof, due to uncertainty. The author then addresses conditions that invalidate a sale: a condition that is not required by the contract, not suitable, provides benefit to one party or the sold item, the beneficiary is worthy, and custom or Sharia does not permit it. Examples of invalid conditions include selling a slave on condition the buyer frees him or on condition the seller uses him for a month, or selling a house on condition the seller lives in it. The text discusses a dispute regarding tailoring and shoe-making conditions: al-Quduri rules it invalid, but al-Kanz allows it for sandals due to custom (istihsan). The final part deals with unknown future dates (Nowruz, Mihrajan, Christian fasting) – the sale is invalid if the term is unknown to the parties. The commentary explains that the Hanafi school distinguishes between fasid (invalid) and batil (void) – here, these sales are fasid, meaning they may become valid if the defect is removed before rejection (e.g., cutting the garment before cancellation). The relied-upon position for the tailoring condition is that it is invalid, but for sandals it is valid due to widespread custom.

Key Terms
المزابنة (al-muzabanah): Selling fresh fruit on trees for dry fruit by estimated measure.
الملامسة (al-mulamasah): A sale where the buyer touches the garment without inspection, obligating the sale with no option.
المنابذة (al-munabadhah): A sale where each party throws his garment to the other without inspection, making the throwing a binding sale.
إلقاء الحجر (ilqa' al-hajar): Sale by throwing a pebble onto garments; whichever garment the pebble lands on is sold without inspection or option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a characteristic of the four prohibited Jahiliyyah sales according to the Hanafi school?
A) They involve ignorance (jahalah) about the sold item.
B) They involve suspending ownership on a chance event (khatar).
C) They are considered batil (void ab initio) and cannot be cured.
D) The Prophet ﷺ prohibited them.

Key Takeaway
The four Jahiliyyah sales—muzabanah, mulamasah, munabadhah, and ilqa' al-hajar—are fasid due to combined jahalah and khatar, but can be cured by later agreement.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Conditions That Invalidate a Sale: The Five Criteria
Listing conditions that invalidate a sale with five criteria

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions that make a contractual condition invalidating (mufsid) and apply them to determine whether a given condition renders a sale fasid.

Core Idea
A condition invalidates a sale only if it is not required by the contract, not suitable, gives benefit to a party, and is neither customary nor Sharia-permitted.

Not every condition added to a sale invalidates it. The Hanafi school sets out five precise criteria to identify when a condition is harmful. The passage lists these criteria and provides examples. This lesson breaks down each criterion and shows how to apply them, so you can distinguish between acceptable conditions (like a payment guarantee) and invalidating ones (like requiring the buyer to free a slave).

Guided Path
1
Identify the five criteria for an invalidating condition
From the text: (1) not required by the contract, (2) not suitable for the contract, (3) provides benefit to one of the contracting parties or to the object of the contract, (4) the beneficiary is worthy of the benefit (ahlu al-istiqhaq), (5) custom ('urf) and Sharia have not permitted it.
These are explicitly listed: 'فلا بد في كون الشرط مفسدا للبيع من هذه الشرائط الخمسة'.
2
Understand each criterion
Criterion 1: The condition is not a natural consequence of the contract (e.g., selling with a guarantee is not required by contract). Criterion 2: It is not harmonious with the contract's purpose (e.g., requiring a service like tailoring is not harmonious with a simple sale). Criterion 3: It bestows a benefit on one party (the buyer or seller) without additional compensation. Criterion 4: The beneficiary is free to receive benefit (e.g., a slave is not worthy). Criterion 5: Neither custom nor Sharia permits this condition.
If any criterion is missing, the condition does not invalidate.
3
Apply to example conditions from the text
The text gives examples: selling a slave on condition that the buyer frees him (invalid because benefit to slave? Actually benefit to buyer of reward? Check text: 'إذا باع عبدا على أن يعتقه المشتري' – it is invalid because it imposes an obligation not inherent in sale. Similarly, condition that seller uses slave for a month, or that buyer lends a dirham, etc. Analyze using the five criteria.
Each example satisfies all five criteria.
4
Differentiate between valid and invalid conditions
The text also mentions conditions that do NOT invalidate: 'شرط الأجل في الثمن' (term in price), 'شرط الخيار' (option clause), 'شرط كفيل بالثمن' (guarantor for price) if accepted. These are either required by contract or suitable or permitted by Sharia.
Also, customary conditions like sandal cutting (discussed in next lesson) are valid due to custom.

كتاب البيوع
أمثلة البيع الفاسد وحكمه
الجواب: يفسد بيع المزابنة والملامسة والمنابذة والبيع بإلقاء الحجر، كما يفسد إذا باع جارية أو دابة واستثنى حملها، أو باع ذراعا من ثوب، أو جذعا من سقف، أو ثوبا من ثوبين.
السؤال: هل يفسد البيع بشروط ؟
الجواب: نعم، يفسد بشرط لا يقتضيه العقد، ولا يلائمه، وفيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين، أو للمعقود عليه، وهو من أهل الاستحقاق، ولم يجر العرف به ولم يرد الشرع بجوازه.
بالقاء الحجر: المزابنة : بيع الرطبة على النخيل بتمر مقطوع مثل كيله تقديرا، ومثله بيع العنب على شجرة الزبيب. والملامسة: هو أن يلمس كل وحد من المتعاقدين ثوب صاحبه من غير تأمل؛ ليلزم اللامس البيع من غير خيار له عند الرؤية، وهذا بأن يكون مثلا في ظلمة، أو يكون الثوب مطويا مرئيا يتفقان على أنه إذا لمسه فقد باعه منه وفساده لتعليق التمليك على أنه متى لمسه وجب البيع وسقط خيار المجلس والمنابذة: أن ينبذ كل واحد منهما ثوبه إلى آخر ولا ينظر كل واحد منهما إلى ثوب صاحبه على جعل النبذ بيعًا، وهذه كانت بيوعا يتعارفونها في الجاهلية.
وإلقاء الحجر أن يلقي حصاة وثمة أثواب، فأي ثوب وقع عليه كان البيع بلا تأمل ورؤية، ولا خيار بعد ذلك، ولا بد أن يسبق تراوضهما يعني تساومهما على الثمن ولا فرق بين كون المبيع معينا أو غير معين، ومعنى النهي ما في كل من الجهالة وتعليق التمليك بالخطر؛ فإنه في معنى إذا وقع حجري على ثوب فقد بعته منك، بعتنيه بكذا، أو إذا نبذته أو لمسته كذا في "الفتح" من "الدر المختار" و"رد المحتار". ولقد نهى النبي عن هذه البيوع، راجع "مشكاة المصابيح" (باب المنهي عنها من البيوع).
واستثنى حملها راجع "منحة الخالق على البحر الرائق" (٩٥/٦). من ثوب في "تنوير الأبصار": وذراع من ثوب يضره التبعيض، قال في "الدر المختار: فلو سلم وقطع قبل فسخ المشتري عاد صحيحا، ولم يضره القطع ككرباس جاز لاندفاع المانع. وفي "الهداية: ولو قطع البائع الذراع أو قلع الجذع قبل أن يفسخ المشتري يعود صحيحا لزوال المفسد من ثوبين البيع فاسد فيه الجهالة المبيع، وكذا يفسد البيع إذا باع من عبدين، كما ذكره في "البحر" (٨٣/٦). بجوازه: قال صاحب البحر" (٩٢/٦) بعد ذكر هذه الشروط فلا بد في كون الشرط مفسدا للبيع من هذه الشرائط الخمسة، فإن كان الشرط يقتضيه العقد، فإنه لا يفسد كشرط أن يحبس المبيع إلى قبض الثمن ونحوه،

Answer: The sale of muzabanah, mulamasah, munabadhah, and the sale by throwing a stone are invalidated, as it is invalidated if one sells a slave girl or an animal and excludes its fetus, or sells a cubit of a garment, or a beam of a roof, or one garment from two garments. Question: Is the sale invalidated by conditions? Answer: Yes, it is invalidated by a condition that is not required by the contract, nor is it suitable for it, and there is benefit in it for one of the two contracting parties, or for the object of the contract, and the beneficiary is worthy of the benefit, and custom has not been established with it, nor has the Sharia permitted it. By throwing a stone: Muzabanah: selling fresh dates on the palm trees for cut dry dates estimated to be the same measure, and likewise selling grapes on the vine for raisins. Mulamasah: that each of the two contracting parties touches the other's garment without reflection; so that the touching obligates the sale with no option for him upon seeing it. This is, for example, in darkness, or if the garment is folded and visible they agree that if he touches it, he has sold it to him. Its invalidity is due to suspending ownership on the condition that whenever he touches it, the sale becomes obligatory and the option of the session falls. Munabadhah: that each of them throws his garment to the other and neither looks at the other's garment, making the throwing a sale. These were sales practiced in the Jahiliyyah. Throwing a stone: that one throws a pebble and there are garments, then whichever garment it falls on, the sale is without reflection and sight, and no option after that. There must be prior negotiation meaning they haggled over the price, and there is no difference whether the sold item is specific or unspecified. The meaning of the prohibition is what is in each of ignorance (jahalah) and suspending ownership to chance (khatar); for it is in the meaning of 'if my stone falls on a garment, I have sold it to you, you have bought it from me for such and such' or 'if I throw it or touch it such', as in al-Fath from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade these sales, see Mishkat al-Masabih (Chapter on Prohibited Sales). Excluding its fetus: see Minhat al-Khaliq ala al-Bahr al-Raiq (6/95). About a cubit of a garment in Tanwir al-Absar: A cubit of a garment harms it by division. He said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: If he delivers and cuts before the buyer cancels, it becomes valid, and cutting does not harm it like cotton cloth (karbas), it is permissible because the impediment is removed. And in al-Hidayah: If the seller cuts the cubit or uproots the beam before the buyer cancels, it becomes valid because the cause of invalidity is removed. From two garments: The sale is invalid due to ignorance of the sold item. Likewise, the sale is invalid if one sells from two slaves, as mentioned in al-Bahr (6/83). About its permissibility: The author of al-Bahr (6/92) said after mentioning these conditions: It is necessary for a condition to be invalidating of the sale to have these five conditions. If the condition is required by the contract, it does not invalidate, like the condition that the seller keep the sold item until receipt of the price, etc. Book of Sales. Examples of invalid sale and its ruling. Question: Explain some conditions that invalidate the sale. Answer: If he sells a slave on condition that the buyer free him, or make him a mudabbar, or make a kitabah, or on condition that the seller use him for a month, or sells a slave girl on condition that the buyer have children from her, or a house on condition that the seller live in it for a known period, or sells an item on condition that the seller not deliver until the beginning of the month, or on condition that the buyer lend him a dirham, or on condition that the buyer give him a gift, then these conditions invalidate the sale. Question: What do you say about a man who buys a garment on condition that the seller cut and sew it as a shirt or a cloak, or buys a sandal on condition that the seller cut it (fashion it) or put a strap on it? Answer: This sale is invalid in these cases. Question: Two men made a sale and the buyer made the price deferred to Nowruz or to Mihrajan and the seller accepted it, or they made the term to the fasting of Christians or the breaking of fast of Jews, what is the ruling of this sale? Answer: This sale is invalid if the two parties do not know these terms. If it is not required by the contract but its validity is established by Sharia then there is no rejection for it, like the condition of a term in the price and in the salam sale, and the condition of option does not invalidate it. If it is customary, like buying a sandal on condition that the seller cut it or put a strap on it, it is permissible. If it is suitable for the sale, it does not invalidate it, like a sale with the condition of a guarantor for the price if he is present and accepts, or if he is absent then comes and accepts before parting. And his statement: 'or there is benefit for the sold item, and it is worthy of (the benefit) by being a human being', so if not, like a condition that the buyer not ride the sold animal, it is not invalidating. From al-Durr al-Mukhtar. 'Straps': this is what al-Quduri chose, and the author of al-Kanz said: The sale of a sandal on condition that he cut it and put a strap on it is valid. The author of al-Bahr said: Qiyas is its invalidity due to the benefit for the buyer while the contract does not require it, and what he mentioned is an answer of istihsan due to custom ('urf), and going against custom involves clear hardship, unlike stipulating the tailoring of a garment due to lack of custom, so it remains on the original qiyas. And nailing a clog (taqbiq) is like putting a strap on a sandal as in Fath al-Qadir, and in al-Bazzaziyyah: He bought a worn-out garment or khuff on condition that the seller patch it, stitch it, and deliver it, it is valid due to custom. And the meaning of yahdhuhu (fashion it) is yqta'uhu (cut it) (6/95). 'These terms': The author of al-Bahr said: That is, the sale is not permissible, it is invalid due to ignorance of the term (ajal), which leads to dispute in the sale because it is based on haggling, unless they both know it because it is known to them, or if the term is to the breaking of fast of Christians =
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is from the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Buyu') and discusses invalid sales (bay' fasid) and conditions that invalidate a sale. It begins by listing four prohibited sales from the Jahiliyyah: muzabanah (selling fresh fruit on trees for dry fruit by estimation), mulamasah (sale by touching without inspection), munabadhah (sale by throwing garments), and sale by throwing a stone (where the garment the stone lands on is sold). All these are invalid due to ignorance (jahalah) and gambling (khatar). The text then explains that an invalid sale also occurs when selling an animal while excluding its fetus, or selling a part of a garment or a beam from a roof, due to uncertainty. The author then addresses conditions that invalidate a sale: a condition that is not required by the contract, not suitable, provides benefit to one party or the sold item, the beneficiary is worthy, and custom or Sharia does not permit it. Examples of invalid conditions include selling a slave on condition the buyer frees him or on condition the seller uses him for a month, or selling a house on condition the seller lives in it. The text discusses a dispute regarding tailoring and shoe-making conditions: al-Quduri rules it invalid, but al-Kanz allows it for sandals due to custom (istihsan). The final part deals with unknown future dates (Nowruz, Mihrajan, Christian fasting) – the sale is invalid if the term is unknown to the parties. The commentary explains that the Hanafi school distinguishes between fasid (invalid) and batil (void) – here, these sales are fasid, meaning they may become valid if the defect is removed before rejection (e.g., cutting the garment before cancellation). The relied-upon position for the tailoring condition is that it is invalid, but for sandals it is valid due to widespread custom.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is defective due to an impermissible condition but can become valid if the condition is removed before cancellation.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A Hanafi juristic preference that abandons qiyas in favor of custom or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following conditions would NOT invalidate a sale according to the Hanafi five criteria?
A) Selling a house on condition that the seller may live in it for one year for free.
B) Selling a car on condition that the buyer must lend the seller $1000.
C) Selling a book on condition that the buyer gives the seller a gift.
D) Selling a shirt on condition that the seller holds it until payment is made.

Key Takeaway
A condition invalidates a sale only if it meets all five criteria: not required, not suitable, provides benefit, beneficiary worthy, and not customary/permitted by Sharia.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
The Tailoring and Sandal Dispute: Qiyas vs. Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute over tailoring and sandal conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a condition to tailor a garment or fashion a sandal invalidates the sale, and evaluate the role of custom (urf) in overriding qiyas via istihsan.

Core Idea
Custom can override qiyas when there is widespread practice and hardship in departure, but not for rare services like tailoring.

The passage presents a classic Hanafi dispute between al-Quduri and al-Kanz on whether a condition that the seller perform a service (cutting/sewing a garment, or cutting/strapping a sandal) invalidates the sale. Al-Quduri says it invalidates via qiyas, while al-Kanz allows the sandal condition due to custom (istihsan). This lesson unpacks the reasoning and shows how the Hanafi school uses istihsan as a source of law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases in the dispute
Case 1: Buying a garment on condition the seller cuts and sews it. Case 2: Buying a sandal on condition the seller cuts and straps it. Al-Quduri says both are invalid (fasid).
The text: 'اشترى ثوبا على أن يقطعه البائع ويخيطه قميصا أو قباء... هذا البيع فاسد'.
2
Recognize the differing position of al-Kanz
The author of al-Kanz says: the sale of a sandal on condition the seller cuts and straps it is valid (jā'iz). This is based on custom (urf) and istihsan.
The text: 'وقال صاحب "الكنز": صح بيع نعل على أن يحذوه ويشركه'.
3
Understand the qiyas argument for invalidity
The original qiyas (general principle) says any condition that provides extra benefit to the buyer (like a service) without additional payment is invalid. Tailoring and sandal cutting both give benefit, so both should be invalid. This is the asl (original case) of tailoring.
The text: 'والقياس فساده لما فيه من النفع للمشتري مع كون العقد لا يقتضيه'.
4
Understand the istihsan argument for validity
Istihsan abandons qiyas due to custom (urf). For sandals, it is customary to buy them and have the seller cut and strap them. Going against this custom would cause hardship (haraj). For tailor-made garments, custom is not established; people usually buy fabric separately.
The text: 'وفي الخروج عن العادة حرج بين بخلاف اشتراط خياطة الثوب لعدم العادة'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The later relied-upon position: for sandals (and similar common items like shoes), the condition is valid due to custom. For garments (tailoring), it remains invalid because custom is not widespread. The author of al-Bahr prefers istihsan for sandal but retains qiyas for tailoring.
Other sources extend custom to patching old garments and nailing clogs (taqbiq).

كتاب البيوع
أمثلة البيع الفاسد وحكمه
الجواب: يفسد بيع المزابنة والملامسة والمنابذة والبيع بإلقاء الحجر، كما يفسد إذا باع جارية أو دابة واستثنى حملها، أو باع ذراعا من ثوب، أو جذعا من سقف، أو ثوبا من ثوبين.
السؤال: هل يفسد البيع بشروط ؟
الجواب: نعم، يفسد بشرط لا يقتضيه العقد، ولا يلائمه، وفيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين، أو للمعقود عليه، وهو من أهل الاستحقاق، ولم يجر العرف به ولم يرد الشرع بجوازه.
بالقاء الحجر: المزابنة : بيع الرطبة على النخيل بتمر مقطوع مثل كيله تقديرا، ومثله بيع العنب على شجرة الزبيب. والملامسة: هو أن يلمس كل وحد من المتعاقدين ثوب صاحبه من غير تأمل؛ ليلزم اللامس البيع من غير خيار له عند الرؤية، وهذا بأن يكون مثلا في ظلمة، أو يكون الثوب مطويا مرئيا يتفقان على أنه إذا لمسه فقد باعه منه وفساده لتعليق التمليك على أنه متى لمسه وجب البيع وسقط خيار المجلس والمنابذة: أن ينبذ كل واحد منهما ثوبه إلى آخر ولا ينظر كل واحد منهما إلى ثوب صاحبه على جعل النبذ بيعًا، وهذه كانت بيوعا يتعارفونها في الجاهلية.
وإلقاء الحجر أن يلقي حصاة وثمة أثواب، فأي ثوب وقع عليه كان البيع بلا تأمل ورؤية، ولا خيار بعد ذلك، ولا بد أن يسبق تراوضهما يعني تساومهما على الثمن ولا فرق بين كون المبيع معينا أو غير معين، ومعنى النهي ما في كل من الجهالة وتعليق التمليك بالخطر؛ فإنه في معنى إذا وقع حجري على ثوب فقد بعته منك، بعتنيه بكذا، أو إذا نبذته أو لمسته كذا في "الفتح" من "الدر المختار" و"رد المحتار". ولقد نهى النبي عن هذه البيوع، راجع "مشكاة المصابيح" (باب المنهي عنها من البيوع).
واستثنى حملها راجع "منحة الخالق على البحر الرائق" (٩٥/٦). من ثوب في "تنوير الأبصار": وذراع من ثوب يضره التبعيض، قال في "الدر المختار: فلو سلم وقطع قبل فسخ المشتري عاد صحيحا، ولم يضره القطع ككرباس جاز لاندفاع المانع. وفي "الهداية: ولو قطع البائع الذراع أو قلع الجذع قبل أن يفسخ المشتري يعود صحيحا لزوال المفسد من ثوبين البيع فاسد فيه الجهالة المبيع، وكذا يفسد البيع إذا باع من عبدين، كما ذكره في "البحر" (٨٣/٦). بجوازه: قال صاحب البحر" (٩٢/٦) بعد ذكر هذه الشروط فلا بد في كون الشرط مفسدا للبيع من هذه الشرائط الخمسة، فإن كان الشرط يقتضيه العقد، فإنه لا يفسد كشرط أن يحبس المبيع إلى قبض الثمن ونحوه،

كتاب البيوع
أمثلة البيع الفاسد وحكمه
السؤال: بينوا بعض الشروط التي تفسد البيع. الجواب: إذا باع عبدا على أن يعتقه المشتري أو يدبره، أو يكاتبه، أو على أن يستخدمه البائع شهرا، أو باع أمة على أن يستولدها المشتري، أو دارا على أن يسكنها البائع مدة معلومة، أو باع عينا على أن لا يسلم البائع إلى رأس الشهر، أو على أن يقرضه المشتري درهما، أو على أن يُهدي له شيئا ، فإن هذه الشروط تفسد البيع. السؤال: وما تقولون في رجل اشترى ثوبا على أن يقطعه البائع ويخيطه قميصا أو قباء، أو اشترى نعلا على أن يحذوها البائع أو يضع عليها شراكا؟
الجواب: هذا البيع فاسد في هذه الصور. السؤال: رجلان تبايعا وجعل المشتري الثمن مؤجلا إلى النيروز أو إلى المهرجان وقبله البائع، أو جعلا الأجل إلى صوم النصارى، أو فطر اليهود، ما حكم هذا البيع ؟
الجواب: هذا البيع فاسد إذا لم يعرف المتبايعان هذه الآجال.
فإن كان لا يقتضيه لكن ثبت تصحيحه شرعا فلا مرد له كشرط الأجل في الثمن وفي المبيع المسلم وشرط الخيار لا يفسده، وإن كان متعارفا كشراء النعل على أن يحذوه البائع أو يشركها فهو جائز، وإن كان ملائما للبيع لا يفسده، كالبيع بشرط كفيل بالثمن إذا كان حاضرا وقبلها، أو غائباً فحضر وقبل قبل التفرق. وقوله: (أو فيه نفع للمبيع، وهو من أهل (الاستحقاق بأن يكون آدميا فلو لم يكن كشرط أن لا يركب الدابة المبيعة لم يكن مفسدا. من "الدر المختار".
شراكا هذا ما اختاره القدوري، وقال صاحب "الكنز": صح بيع نعل على أن يحذوه ويشركه. قال صاحب "البحر": والقياس فساده لما فيه من النفع للمشتري مع كون العقد لا يقتضيه، وما ذكره جواب الاستحسان للتعامل، وفي الخروج عن العادة حرج بين بخلاف اشتراط خياطة الثوب لعدم العادة فبقي على أصل القياس، وتسمير القبقاب كتشريك النعل كما في "فتح القدير"، وفي "البزازية" : اشترى ثوبا أو خفا خلقا على أن يرقعه البائع ويخرزه ويسلمه صح للعرف، ومعنى يحذوه يقطعه (٩٥/٦). هذه الآجال: قال صاحب "البحر": أي لا يجوز البيع، وهو فاسد لجهالة الأجل، وهي مفضية إلى المنازعة في البيع لابتنائها على المماكسة، إلا إذا كانا يعرفانه لكونه معلوما عندهما، أو كان التأجيل إلى فطر النصارى =

Commentary
This passage is from the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Buyu') and discusses invalid sales (bay' fasid) and conditions that invalidate a sale. It begins by listing four prohibited sales from the Jahiliyyah: muzabanah (selling fresh fruit on trees for dry fruit by estimation), mulamasah (sale by touching without inspection), munabadhah (sale by throwing garments), and sale by throwing a stone (where the garment the stone lands on is sold). All these are invalid due to ignorance (jahalah) and gambling (khatar). The text then explains that an invalid sale also occurs when selling an animal while excluding its fetus, or selling a part of a garment or a beam from a roof, due to uncertainty. The author then addresses conditions that invalidate a sale: a condition that is not required by the contract, not suitable, provides benefit to one party or the sold item, the beneficiary is worthy, and custom or Sharia does not permit it. Examples of invalid conditions include selling a slave on condition the buyer frees him or on condition the seller uses him for a month, or selling a house on condition the seller lives in it. The text discusses a dispute regarding tailoring and shoe-making conditions: al-Quduri rules it invalid, but al-Kanz allows it for sandals due to custom (istihsan). The final part deals with unknown future dates (Nowruz, Mihrajan, Christian fasting) – the sale is invalid if the term is unknown to the parties. The commentary explains that the Hanafi school distinguishes between fasid (invalid) and batil (void) – here, these sales are fasid, meaning they may become valid if the defect is removed before rejection (e.g., cutting the garment before cancellation). The relied-upon position for the tailoring condition is that it is invalid, but for sandals it is valid due to widespread custom.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A Hanafi juristic preference that abandons qiyas in favor of custom or necessity.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive rulings for new cases from existing ones.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom – a recognized practice that can influence rulings in the absence of explicit texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Conditions That Invalidate a Sale
Lists examples of conditions that make a sale invalid (fasid).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify conditions that make a sale fasid in Hanafi fiqh and state the general principle that invalid conditions give extra benefit to one party beyond the contract's requirements.

Core Idea
A sale becomes fasid when a condition grants personal benefit to one party outside the contract's necessities.

This passage presents several examples of conditions that render a sale invalid (fasid) in the Hanafi school. The common thread is that each condition provides an extra benefit to one party — the buyer or seller — that is not part of the essential exchange. Understanding these examples helps you grasp the key rule: any condition that is not inherent in the sale contract and gives a benefit to either party will corrupt the sale. The Hanafis classify such sales as fasid (irregular), meaning the core contract is sound but the condition must be removed for the sale to become valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of conditions
Go through each condition mentioned: freeing a slave, making mudabbar, kitaba, seller's use for a month, seeking children from a female slave, seller living in a house, delayed delivery, lending money, giving a gift.
All conditions are additional stipulations beyond price and delivery.
2
Identify the common characteristic
Each condition provides a direct benefit to either the seller (e.g., using the slave, living in the house) or the buyer (e.g., freeing the slave, seeking children). This benefit is not required by the sale itself.
The benefit is personal, not inherent in the contract.
3
Understand the ruling (fasid)
Because the condition adds an extra benefit, the sale becomes fasid (irregular). The contract is not void (batil), but the corrupt condition must be removed for the sale to be valid.
Hanafis distinguish fasid from batil: fasid can be cured.
4
Apply the rule to new examples
If you encounter a sale with a condition, ask: does this condition give a benefit to either party that is not part of the contract? If yes, the sale is fasid.
Customary conditions (e.g., warranty, payment deadline) are exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمثلة البيع الفاسد وحكمه السؤال: بينوا بعض الشروط التي تفسد البيع. الجواب: إذا باع عبدا على أن يعتقه المشتري أو يدبره، أو يكاتبه، أو على أن يستخدمه البائع شهرا، أو باع أمة على أن يستولدها المشتري، أو دارا على أن يسكنها البائع مدة معلومة، أو باع عينا على أن لا يسلم البائع إلى رأس الشهر، أو على أن يقرضه المشتري درهما، أو على أن يُهدي له شيئا ، فإن هذه الشروط تفسد البيع.
Examples of invalid sales and their ruling. Question: Mention some conditions that invalidate the sale. Answer: If he sells a slave on the condition that the buyer sets him free, or makes him a mudabbar, or makes a kitaba with him; or on the condition that the seller uses him for a month; or sells a female slave on the condition that the buyer seeks children from her; or a house on the condition that the seller lives in it for a known period; or sells an item on the condition that the seller does not deliver until the beginning of the month; or on the condition that the buyer lends him a dirham, or gives him a gift – then these conditions invalidate the sale.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage lists several examples of conditions that render a sale invalid (fasid) in the Hanafi school. The common thread is that each condition provides a benefit to one party that is not inherent in the sale contract itself. For example, selling a slave with the condition that the buyer emancipates him adds a contract of servitude beyond the sale, which is not required. Similarly, requiring the seller to use the slave for a month or the buyer to have children from a female slave are external conditions. The ruling is clear: such conditions invalidate the sale. The Hanafis call this type of sale fasid (irregular) because the contract's core is sound but the condition corrupts it. The condition can potentially be dropped to validate the sale, as discussed later.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is not valid due to a defect that can be removed, making it valid retroactively.
شرط (shart): A stipulation in a contract that provides benefit to one party and is not inherent in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Selling food on the condition that the seller delivers it fresh.

Key Takeaway
A sale becomes invalid (fasid) when a condition provides a personal benefit to either party that is not required by the contract itself.

Sale with Condition of Additional Work: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional sale with additional work

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a conditional sale of slippers but not cloth, applying the distinction between qiyas and istihsan based on custom.

Core Idea
Custom (ta'amul) can override qiyas to validate a conditional sale if departing from custom causes hardship.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a seller's promise to tailor cloth or make slippers invalidates the sale. The disagreement arises from the tension between strict analogy (qiyas) and juristic preference (istihsan) grounded in customary practice. You will learn how Hanafi jurists reconcile these principles and identify the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Transaction and Condition
Recognize what is being sold (cloth or slippers) and what extra work the seller must perform (cutting, sewing, making, strapping, patching). The condition provides a benefit to the buyer not inherent in a simple sale.
The condition is an additional obligation on the seller beyond delivery of the item.
2
State the Default Ruling and Its Basis (Qiyas)
According to qiyas, the sale is fasid because the contract does not require the seller to perform work; the condition gives the buyer extra benefit without additional consideration in the price, resembling gharar or riba.
Al-Quduri and the general analogy hold this view.
3
Recognize the Istihsan Argument and Custom
Al-Kanz and later authorities argue for validity based on istihsan. The widespread custom (ta'amul) of such transactions removes the objection because people regularly engage in them without hardship. Departing from custom would cause undue difficulty.
Istihsan is a stronger proof than qiyas when supported by custom.
4
Differentiate Between Items Based on Custom
The author of al-Bahr reconciles: tailoring cloth is not customary, so invalidity remains; making slippers and nailing clogs are customary, so validity applies. Patching old khuffs also falls under custom per al-Bazzaziyya.
The custom must be established for that specific type of work.
5
Confirm the Mufta Bihi Position
The relied-upon position is that of al-Kanz: sales with conditions of customary additional work are valid. This is supported by later fatwa collections like Fath al-Qadir and al-Bazzaziyya.
For non-customary work, the sale remains fasid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: وما تقولون في رجل اشترى ثوبا على أن يقطعه البائع ويخيطه قميصا أو قباء، أو اشترى نعلا على أن يحذوها البائع أو يضع عليها شراكا؟ الجواب: هذا البيع فاسد في هذه الصور. شراكا هذا ما اختاره القدوري، وقال صاحب "الكنز": صح بيع نعل على أن يحذوه ويشركه. قال صاحب "البحر": والقياس فساده لما فيه من النفع للمشتري مع كون العقد لا يقتضيه، وما ذكره جواب الاستحسان للتعامل، وفي الخروج عن العادة حرج بين بخلاف اشتراط خياطة الثوب لعدم العادة فبقي على أصل القياس، وتسمير القبقاب كتشريك النعل كما في "فتح القدير"، وفي "البزازية" : اشترى ثوبا أو خفا خلقا على أن يرقعه البائع ويخرزه ويسلمه صح للعرف، ومعنى يحذوه يقطعه (٩٥/٦).
Question: What do you say regarding a man who buys cloth on condition that the seller cuts and sews it into a shirt or a cloak, or buys a slipper on condition that the seller makes it (or puts a strap on it)? Answer: This sale is invalid in these forms. The word 'shiraka' is what al-Quduri chose. The author of al-Kanz said: The sale of a slipper on condition that the seller makes it and puts a strap is valid. The author of al-Bahr said: The qiyas is its invalidity because it contains benefit for the buyer while the contract does not require it. What he mentioned is a response of istihsan due to custom. Departing from custom involves clear hardship, unlike stipulating tailoring of cloth because there is no custom, so it remains on the original qiyas. Nailing clogs is like putting a strap on a slipper as in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Bazzaziyya: If he buys a cloth or old khuff on condition that the seller patches it, tans it, and delivers, it is valid due to custom. The meaning of 'yahdhuha' is to cut it.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a conditional sale where the seller is required to perform additional work on the sold item. Al-Quduri ruled that the sale is fasid because the condition provides benefit to the buyer beyond the contract. However, the author of al-Kanz considered it valid if such conditions are customary. The author of al-Bahr explains that while qiyas (analogy) would render it invalid, istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom validates it due to the hardship of abandoning customary practice. The difference between tailoring cloth and making slippers is that tailoring cloth is not customary, so the general rule of invalidity remains; but making slippers and nailing clogs are customary, hence valid. Later texts like al-Bazzaziyya extend this to patching old khuffs. This illustrates the important Hanafi principle that custom can override qiyas to facilitate transactions.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that sets aside the result of qiyas due to a stronger indication from custom, necessity, or public good.
تعامل (ta'amul): Widespread customary practice of people in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author of al-Kanz consider the sale of a slipper with a condition that the seller puts a strap to be valid?
A) Because qiyas directly permits it.
B) Because it is based on istihsan due to widespread custom.
C) Because the condition is inherent in the contract.
D) Because the buyer benefits from the condition.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, custom (ta'amul) can override qiyas to validate conditional sales that would otherwise be invalid, provided the condition is customary and does not involve prohibitions.

Sale with Deferred Payment to Unknown Festivals
Ruling on sale with deferred payment to Nowruz, Mihrajan, Christian fasting, or Jewish breaking of fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale where payment is deferred to an unknown festival and explain why it is fasid due to jahalah (ignorance) of the term.

Core Idea
A sale deferred to an unknown date is fasid — uncertainty in the payment term invalidates the contract.

This lesson examines a specific case from Hanafi fiqh: a sale where the buyer agrees to pay at a future time tied to a festival (like Nowruz) or a religious observance (like Christian fasting). The key question is whether such a term is known enough to avoid gharar (uncertainty). We will see how the Hanafi school distinguishes between known and unknown terms, and why the sale is ruled fasid when the term is ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a credit sale where the payment term is tied to a specific festival or observance. The ruling is that the sale is fasid (invalid) if the term is unknown to the parties.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to prohibited sales involving gharar (uncertainty). The uncertainty in the payment date leads to potential disputes, so the sale is invalid by analogy.
The dalil is aqli (rational) rather than naqli (textual).
3
Understand the condition of knowledge
The sale is fasid due to jahalah (ignorance) of the term. The condition for validity is that the payment term must be known to both parties at the time of contract.
If the term is known (e.g., Nowruz is known as the first day of summer), the sale is valid.
4
Apply the condition to examples
Examine the examples: Christian fasting (Lent) is not fixed to a specific calendar date and is generally unknown, so it causes jahalah. Jewish breaking of fast (post-Yom Kippur) is known as it occurs on a fixed date. Nowruz and Mihrajan are known to those familiar with Persian culture.
The ruling depends on the parties' knowledge, not the festivals' inherent nature.
5
Note the exception and practical effect
If the parties know the exact meaning of the term (e.g., Nowruz is the spring equinox), the sale is valid. The fasid sale can be rectified by agreeing on a known term later.
The fasid ruling does not make the contract batil; it can be corrected by clarifying the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: رجلان تبايعا وجعل المشتري الثمن مؤجلا إلى النيروز أو إلى المهرجان وقبله البائع، أو جعلا الأجل إلى صوم النصارى، أو فطر اليهود، ما حكم هذا البيع ؟ الجواب: هذا البيع فاسد إذا لم يعرف المتبايعان هذه الآجال. فإن كان لا يقتضيه لكن ثبت تصحيحه شرعا فلا مرد له كشرط الأجل في الثمن وفي المبيع المسلم وشرط الخيار لا يفسده، وإن كان متعارفا كشراء النعل على أن يحذوه البائع أو يشركها فهو جائز، وإن كان ملائما للبيع لا يفسده، كالبيع بشرط كفيل بالثمن إذا كان حاضرا وقبلها، أو غائباً فحضر وقبل قبل التفرق. وقوله: (أو فيه نفع للمبيع، وهو من أهل (الاستحقاق بأن يكون آدميا فلو لم يكن كشرط أن لا يركب الدابة المبيعة لم يكن مفسدا. من "الدر المختار". هذه الآجال: قال صاحب "البحر": أي لا يجوز البيع، وهو فاسد لجهالة الأجل، وهي مفضية إلى المنازعة في البيع لابتنائها على المماكسة، إلا إذا كانا يعرفانه لكونه معلوما عندهما، أو كان التأجيل إلى فطر النصارى = من يوم بعدما شروعوا في صومهم بالأيام؛ لأن صومهم بالأيام معلوم فلا جهالة فيه، والنيروز أول يوم الصيف، وهو أول يوم تحل فيه الشمس الحمل والمهرجان أول يوم من الشتاء، وهو أول تحل فيه الشمس الميزان كذا في "السراج الوهاج"، ثم قال صاحب "البحر": وإنما حُصَّ الصوم بالنصارى والفطر باليهود؛ لأن صوم النصارى غير معلوم وفطرهم معلوم، واليهود بعكسه، مع أنه إذا باع إلى صوم اليهود، فالحكم كذلك لا يتفاوت فيكون المعنى إلى صوم النصارى وفطرهم، وإلى فطر اليهود وصومهم، فاكتفى بذكر أحدهما. (٩٦/٦). بن قال الشامي في رد المحتار: أصله نوروز عُرِّب، وقد تكلم به عمر رضي الله عنه فقال كل يوم نيرزونا حين كان الكفار يبتهجون به والمهرجان بكسر الميم وسكون الهاء وفي "القهستاني" أنه نوعان عامة: وهو أول يوم من الخريف، أعني اليوم السادس عشر من "مهر" ماه وخاصة وهو اليوم السادس والعشرون منه. قلت: هذا الذي روي عن عمر رضي الله عنه، قاله علي رضي الله عنه أيضا، فقد ذكر الخطيب في "تاريخ بغداد" (٣٢٦/١٣): النعمان المرزبان أبو ثابت (جد الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمه الله) هو الذي أهدى لعلي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه الفالوذج في يوم النيروز، فقال: نيرزونا كل يوم، وقيل: كان ذلك في المهرجان فقال: مهرجونا كل يوم.
Question: Two men made a sale and the buyer made the payment deferred to Nowruz or to Mihrajan and the seller accepted it, or they set the time to the fast of Christians or the breaking of fast of Jews – what is the ruling of this sale? Answer: This sale is invalid if the two parties do not know these times. If the condition is not required by the contract but its validation is established by the Law, there is no way to reject it, like stipulating a term for payment or for the item sold (salam) or stipulating an option – that does not invalidate. And if it is customary, like buying a slipper on condition that the seller makes it or puts a strap – it is permissible. And if it is suitable for the sale, it does not invalidate, like the sale with a condition of a guarantor for the payment if the seller is present and accepts it, or if absent then he attends and accepts before separation. And his statement: 'Or it contains benefit for the sold item, and it is one deserving it, i.e., a human; if not, like a condition not to ride the sold animal – it is not invalidating.' From al-Durr al-Mukhtar. These terms: The author of al-Bahr said: i.e., the sale is not permissible, and it is fasid due to ignorance of the term, which leads to dispute because bargaining is based on it, unless they both know it because it is known to them, or if the deferment is to Christians' breaking of fast, from the day after they begin their fasting with days, because their fasting is with known days, so there is no ignorance. Nowruz is the first day of summer, the first day the sun enters Aries; Mihrajan is the first day of winter, the first day the sun enters Libra. So in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Then the author of al-Bahr said: The reason why fasting was specified with Christians and breaking fast with Jews is that Christians' fasting is not known but their breaking fast is known, and the Jews are the opposite. However, if he sells up to the fast of the Jews, the ruling is the same, no difference; the meaning is up to Christians' fasting and their breaking fast, and up to Jews' breaking fast and their fasting, so mentioning one suffices. (96/6). Ibn (?) al-Shami in Radd al-Muhtar said: Its origin is Nowruz, Arabized. Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) spoke of it and said 'Every day is Nowruz' when the disbelievers were rejoicing in it. Mihrajan is with kasr of the meem and sukoon of the ha. In al-Qahistani it is of two types: general – which is the first day of autumn, meaning the 16th of the month of Mihr; and specific – which is the 26th of it. I say: This is what was narrated from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him); Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) also said it. Al-Khatib in Tarikh Baghdad (13/326) mentioned: Al-Nu'man al-Marzaban Abu Thabit (the grandfather of Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him) is the one who gifted 'fālūdhaj' to Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) on the day of Nowruz, so he said, 'Nowruz us every day,' and it was said that it was on the day of Mihrajan, so he said, 'Mihrajan us every day.'
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of sales where payment is deferred to specific uncertain time periods such as Nowruz, Mihrajan, Christian fasting, or Jewish breaking of fast. The ruling is straightforward: if the parties do not know these times, the sale is fasid due to ignorance (jahalah) of the term, which leads to dispute. However, if both parties know the exact meaning (e.g., Nowruz is the first day of summer when the sun enters Aries), the sale is valid. The text further distinguishes between conditions: some conditions are valid because they are either (1) part of the contract like deferred payment in salam, (2) customary, or (3) suitable for the sale (e.g., guaranteeing the price). A lengthy explanation by the author of al-Bahr clarifies why Christian fasting is considered unknown (it varies) while Jewish breaking of fast (after Yom Kippur?) is known, and vice versa. The anecdote about Umar and Ali shows that the Companions did not emphasize these festivals as particularly significant. Overall, the key principle is that a sale must be free from uncertainty about the payment date to be valid.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): The fixed term for payment in a credit sale.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty that leads to invalidity of a contract.
النيروز (al-nayruz): Persian New Year, the first day of summer (spring equinox).
المهرجان (al-mihrajan): Persian festival, first day of autumn (autumnal equinox).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it involves non-Muslim practices.
B) Because Christian fasting is not a fixed day and is generally unknown.
C) Because the parties did not stipulate a specific day.
D) Because the seller did not accept the condition.

Key Takeaway
A sale deferred to an unknown term is fasid; the term must be known to both parties to avoid gharar and ensure contract validity.

Sale Deferred to Natural Events: The Ruling of Fasid due to Jahalah
Ruling on sale with payment deferred to natural events like harvest, threshing, grape-picking, or arrival of pilgrims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sales deferred to uncertain natural events (harvest, threshing, grape-picking, pilgrimage), identify the illah (cause) of the ruling, and apply the principle of jahalah in payment terms.

Core Idea
A sale deferred to an uncertain natural event is fasid because timing is not fixed.

In the previous lessons, we discussed the requirements for a valid deferred payment sale. One key condition is that the payment term must be fixed and known. This passage examines cases where the term is tied to natural events such as harvest, threshing, grape-picking, or the arrival of pilgrims. The Hanafi school rules these sales fasid because the exact timing of these events is unpredictable—they can occur early or late—leading to uncertainty (jahalah) in the payment obligation. Let us delve into the ruling, the reasoning behind it, and the lexical notes provided by the jurist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the question–answer format: the answer states that the sale is 'fasid' (invalid) in all four cases. Note the ruling applies uniformly to harvest, threshing, grape-picking, and arrival of pilgrims.
The ruling is not merely 'invalid' but specifically fasid (defective), which in Hanafi fiqh means the contract is not entirely void but can be corrected if the defect is removed.
2
Understand the illah (effective cause)
The author provides the reason: 'because they (these events) can be early or late, so the term is not fixed.' This is the illah: uncertainty due to variability. The illah is rational (dalil aqli) — no text is cited, only the inherent ambiguity.
In Hanafi usul, if a condition (here, fixed term) is missing, the contract becomes fasid. The illah is the absence of certainty (gharar in time).
3
Analyze the lexical clarifications
The author defines 'hasad', 'qataf', and 'diyas' to confirm that these are names of activities, not fixed times. The lexical note about 'diyas' (threshing) being derived from sword polishing shows jurists use the term metaphorically, but it still denotes a process, not a calendar date.
This clarifies that the event itself, not the word, is what matters. The uncertainty lies in the occurrence of the event, not in the meaning.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
Consider other uncertain events: 'when the fruit ripens', 'when it rains', 'when the snow melts'. The same illah applies — the term is not fixed, so the sale is fasid. Conversely, if the term is tied to a fixed calendar date (e.g., 1 Muharram), it is valid.
The distinguishing factor is whether the event has a fixed or variable occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: وهل يجوز البيع إذا جعلا الأجل لأداء الثمن الحصاد والدياس والقطاف وقدوم الحاج؟ الجواب: البيع فاسد في هذه الصور. الحصاد بكسر الحاء وفتحها أراد به قطع الزرع عن الأرض، والقطاف قطع العنب من أشجارها، أن يوطأ بقوائم الدواب حتى يصير تبنا وتخرج الحبوب منها. قال المطرزي في المغرب: الدياس صقل السيف واستعمال الفقهاء إياه في موضع الدياسة جائز. وإنما لم يجز البيع إلى هذه الآجال؛ لأنها تتقدم وتتأخر، فلا يتعين الأجل.
Question: Is the sale permissible if they set the term for payment to harvest, threshing, grape-picking, or arrival of the pilgrim? Answer: The sale is invalid in these forms. 'Hasad' (with kasr or fatha of ha) means cutting the crop from the ground; 'qataf' means cutting grapes from their vines; that it is trodden by the feet of animals until it becomes straw and grains come out of it. Al-Mutarrizi in al-Mughrib said: 'Diyas' originally means polishing the sword, and it is permissible for jurists to use it in the sense of threshing. The sale is not valid to these terms because they can occur early or late, so the term is not fixed.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage continues the theme of deferred payment to uncertain times. Natural events like harvest (hasad), threshing (diyas), and grape-picking (qataf) are not fixed to a specific date; they vary with climate and conditions. Likewise, the arrival of pilgrims (qudum al-hajj) is also variable. The ruling is that such a sale is fasid due to the uncertainty (jahalah) of the payment date. The author provides lexical definitions: hasad refers to the act of cutting crops, qataf is cutting grapes, and threshing is the process of separating grain from chaff. Even though the primary meaning of 'diyas' is sword polishing, usage allows it for threshing. The underlying reasoning is that because these events can be early or late, the term is ambiguous, leading to potential dispute. Hence, the sale is invalid.

Key Terms
الحصاد (al-hasad): The harvest season for crops; varies with climate.
الدياس (al-diyas): Threshing, separating grain from straw, originally meaning polishing a sword.
القطاف (al-qataf): Grape-picking season.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a sale deferred to 'harvest' considered fasid?
A) Because harvest is a religious event.
B) Because the harvest time can vary, causing uncertainty.
C) Because the word is ambiguous in meaning.
D) Because the harvest is not owned by the seller.

Key Takeaway
A sale with payment deferred to a natural event like harvest is fasid because the term is uncertain, and uncertainty (jahalah) in payment terms invalidates the contract.

Curing a Fasid Sale by Dropping the Condition
Q&A ruling on curing a fasid sale by dropping the condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on curing a fasid sale by dropping the invalid condition before its time and apply it to a transaction scenario.

Core Idea
A fasid sale becomes valid if the invalid condition is dropped before it takes effect.

Sometimes a sale contract contains an invalid condition—like an uncertain payment deadline—that makes the contract fasid (corrupt). But does that mean the whole contract is doomed? The Hanafi school allows a cure: if both parties mutually agree to remove the condition before it occurs, the contract becomes valid (ja'iz) immediately. This lesson examines that ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Defect
First, recognize what made the sale fasid: an invalid condition (shart fasid) that was inserted into the contract. Common examples include a condition to deliver at an unknown time or a condition that gives one party an unfair advantage.
Not all conditions invalidate; only those that conflict with the nature of the contract.
2
Check the Timing of the Drop
The cure works only if the condition is dropped before its time arrives. If the condition has already occurred or failed, the contract remains fasid.
The condition must be dropped before it is due or fulfilled.
3
Confirm Mutual Consent
Both parties must agree to drop the condition. Unilateral dropping does not cure the contract. The agreement can be express or implied (e.g., by conduct).
Mutual consent is essential for the cure.
4
Conclude the Ruling
Once the condition is dropped with mutual consent before its time, the contract becomes valid (ja'iz) automatically. No need to re-execute the contract or repeat the offer and acceptance.
The underlying sale is sound; only the condition was corrupt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال: جعلا هذه الآجال بينهما ثم تراضيا بإسقاطها قبل حلولها، هل ينقلب العقد جائزا في هذه الصورة؟ الجواب: نعم، ينقلب جائزا.
Question: If they set these terms between themselves and then mutually agree to drop them before they occur, does the contract become valid in this case? Answer: Yes, it becomes valid.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This short passage provides the remedy for a fasid sale: if the invalid condition (such as a deferred payment to an uncertain time) is voluntarily dropped by both parties before its time, the contract becomes valid (ja'iz). The condition was the only defect; once removed, the underlying sale is sound. This illustrates the Hanafi concept that a fasid contract is curable if the defect can be eliminated. The contract does not need to be re-executed; it becomes valid by mere mutual agreement to drop the condition before its occurrence.

Key Terms
إسقاط (isqat): The act of dropping or removing a condition from a contract.
فاسد (fasid): A contract that is defective due to an invalid condition or missing essential element, but the underlying contract is valid in essence.
جائز (ja'iz): Permissible, valid; the contract is enforceable and produces its legal effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a fasid sale become valid according to this passage?
A) If the condition is fulfilled.
B) If the parties regret the condition.
C) If the condition is dropped before its time by mutual consent.
D) If a new contract is drawn up.

Key Takeaway
A fasid sale can be cured by dropping the invalid condition before it occurs, provided both parties agree; no new contract is needed.

Permissibility of Selling Dogs, Leopards, and Beasts of Prey
Ruling that it is permissible to sell dogs, leopards, and other predators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling dogs and beasts of prey and explain the basis for its permissibility.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits selling dogs and predators because they have permissible uses.

This brief passage comes from a Hanafi legal text answering whether selling dogs, leopards, and other predatory animals is permissible. The answer is a clear 'yes.' This ruling may surprise those familiar with the Shafi'i or Hanbali positions, which consider dogs impure. However, the Hanafi school looks at the potential benefit of these animals, such as guarding or hunting, and sees no prohibition against trading them. This lesson will help you anchor the Hanafi view on this transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of dogs, leopards, and beasts of prey (siba'). The ruling given is ja'iz (permissible).
2
Note the generality of the ruling
The text does not impose conditions such as training or domestication; the default is permissibility.
Later Hanafi scholars may restrict based on necessity of benefit.
3
Contrast with other schools
Recognize that the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools generally prohibit the sale of dogs because they consider them najis (impure). The Hanafi position is based on the animals' permissible uses.
This ikhtilaf is rooted in different usul regarding najasah and manfa'ah.
4
Understand the Hanafi legal basis
The permissibility is derived from the principle that all transactions are mubah unless explicitly prohibited. Since there is no clear text forbidding the sale of these animals, and they have benefit, the sale is valid.
This is also supported by istihsan if needed.

Source Text

Commentary
This short question and answer affirm the Hanafi position that selling dogs, leopards, and other predatory animals (siba') is permissible. This differs from some other schools that consider dogs impure and thus unsalable. The Hanafi reasoning is that these animals have benefit (such as guarding, hunting, or show) and thus can be traded. The ruling is general and covers both trained and untrained animals, though later scholars may apply conditions of benefit.

Uncertain Payment Term Invalidates the Sale but Can Be Cured
States the invalidity of sale with uncertain payment term and its cure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why a sale with an uncertain payment term is fāsid and explain how the parties can rectify it.

Core Idea
A sale with an uncertain payment term is fāsid, but the parties can rectify it by dropping the condition before the term arrives.

Imagine selling a product with payment due ‘when the harvest comes’ – is that valid? In Hanafi fiqh, such a term is considered unknown (majhūl), making the contract invalid. However, the law provides a remedy: if the parties agree to remove the uncertain term before it matures, the contract becomes valid. This lesson explores the ruling and its underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale with payment tied to an uncertain event (e.g., harvest). The ruling: the sale is fāsid.
2
Understand the cause of invalidity
The cause is jahālah (uncertainty) in the term, which prevents the contract from being binding.
Hanafis require the term to be fixed and known at contract formation.
3
Recognize the distinction between fāsid and bātil
Fāsid means the contract is invalid due to a flaw that can be cured; bātil is irreparably void. Here, the sale is fāsid, not bātil.
4
Learn the cure
If the parties drop the uncertain condition before the event occurs, the contract becomes valid (jā’iz).
The cure must happen before the term arrives; after arrival, the condition is already violated.
5
Apply the principle to other examples
The same ruling applies to terms like threshing, grape-picking, arrival of pilgrims, and fasts of Christians or Jews – any event that can vary in time.
The commentary also mentions Persian festivals (Nowruz, Mihrajan) as examples of fixed terms.

الجواب: البيع فاسد في هذه الصور.

Book of Sales. Examples of Invalid Sale and its Ruling. Question: Is the sale valid if they make the term for payment of the price the harvest, threshing, grape-picking, and the arrival of pilgrims? Answer: The sale is invalid in these cases. Question: If they made these terms between them and then mutually agreed to drop them before they arrive, does the contract become valid in this case? Nowruz is the first day of summer, which is the first day the sun enters Aries, and Mihrajan is the first day of winter, which is the first day the sun enters Libra. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Even if one sells to the Jews' fast, the ruling is the same and does not differ; so the meaning is to the Christians' fast and breaking of fast, and to the Jews' breaking of fast and fast, so mentioning one sufficed. The sale is not permitted to these terms because they come early and late, so the term is not fixed.

This passage discusses the invalidity of a sale where the payment term is tied to events that are uncertain in timing. The first question asks about selling with payment due at harvest, threshing, grape-picking, or the arrival of pilgrims. The answer is that the sale is fāsid (invalid) because these events are unpredictable; they can occur earlier or later, causing uncertainty (jahālah) in the term. This is a fundamental principle in Hanafi fiqh: a sale with an uncertain term is invalid. The second question asks if the parties can later agree to drop the condition and make the sale valid. The ruling is yes, because the contract was initially fāsid due to the condition, and removing it before the term arrives eliminates the cause of invalidity. The commentary then expands on why sales tied to Christian fasts or Jewish fasts are treated similarly, and explains the Persian festivals Nowruz and Mihrajan, with historical anecdotes. The key takeaway is that any term that is not fixed in time renders the sale invalid, but the parties can cure it by mutual agreement to cancel the condition before it is due.

Selling Dogs and Predators: Hanafi Ruling
Simple ruling statement on sale of dogs/predators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling dogs and predatory animals and explain why it differs from the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
In the Hanafi school, selling dogs and predators is permissible because they have benefit.

At first glance, dogs are considered najis (impure) in Islamic law. Yet the Hanafi school permits their sale, along with leopards and other predatory animals. This ruling rests on the principle that any animal that has lawful benefit can be sold, regardless of its physical impurity. In this lesson, we examine the ruling and the key difference with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the Arabic text: the question asks about selling dogs, leopards, and predators; the answer is 'yes, it is permissible'. Note the word 'jā'iz'.
This is a clear ruling statement without conditions.
2
Understand the Basis of Permissibility
The commentary explains that the permissibility is based on benefit. Dogs can be trained for hunting, guarding, etc. Leopards were also used for hunting.
Benefit (manfa'ah) makes an object valuable in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i Position
The Shafi'i school prohibits selling dogs because they are impure. The Hanafi school separates impurity (najāsa) from commodity value (māliyya). Impurity does not negate benefit.
This is a key ikhtilaf: impurity vs benefit.
4
Identify the Authority Level
The ruling is from ẓāhir al-riwāyah, the core Hanafi texts. It is the muftā bihī (relied-upon) position.
No intra-Hanafi dispute is recorded on this.

Source Text

Question: Is the sale of a dog, a leopard, and predators permissible? Answer: Yes, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This is a standalone ruling in the Hanafi school that selling dogs, leopards, and other predatory animals is permissible (jā'iz). This is despite the fact that dogs are generally considered impure (najis). The permissibility is based on the fact that these animals have a benefit—dogs can be used for hunting, guarding, etc. The Hanafi school does not consider the sale of dogs to be prohibited, unlike the Shafi'i school which prohibits it. This ruling is from the ẓāhir al-riwāyah and is the relied-upon position.

الفهد (al-fahd): A leopard; a predatory animal used for hunting.
